Major Sins: Definition and Criteria in Hanafi Usul
Enumeration and theoretical definition of major sins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a major sin (kabīrah) according to Hanafi usul, identify the three criteria (decisive text, hadd/curse/threat, mafsada), and apply the rule that the list is not exhaustive.

Core Idea
A major sin is any act whose corruption equals or exceeds that of explicitly named kabā'ir – the list is illustrative, not exhaustive.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut presents the Hanafi usul approach to defining major sins. It begins with a list of kabā'ir from Companions, then pivots to the theoretical criteria. The key insight: the enumeration is not limited; any act causing similar or greater harm (mafsada) is also a major sin. The passage also discusses the role of repentance and persistence in minor sins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the enumerated major sins
Read the list attributed to Ibn 'Umar, Abu Hurayra, and 'Ali, and note additions like yamin ghamus and persistence.
Some additions like theft are disputed in transmission.
2
Understand the criteria for a major sin
The text presents three criteria: (1) prohibited by decisive text (nass qat'i), (2) carries a hadd, curse, or threat, and (3) has mafsada equal to or greater than the mentioned sins.
The majority favor the second criterion.
3
Grasp the mafsada extension
The chosen view is that enumeration is not for limitation. Any act with equal or greater mafsada, even if not listed, is a major sin.
Ibn Abbas reported up to 700 major sins.
4
Apply the criteria to examples
The text gives analogies: non-khamr intoxicants like khamr, liwat like zina, hurting teacher like hurting parents. Also greater sins: highway robbery, aiding enemies.
This shows how to analogize new sins.
5
Note the role of repentance and persistence
The hadith 'no major sin with repentance, no minor sin with persistence' teaches that minor sins become major through persistence, and major sins are forgiven with repentance.
This affects classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكبائر فعن ابن عمر الشرك بالله تعالى ( والقتل) عمداً من غير حق. وقذف المحصنة، والزنا والفرار من الزحف أي من مقابلة العدو الكافر الحربي لكن إذا لم يكن المسلمون أقل من النصف (والسحر) أي تعلمه والعمل به وبعضهم أباحوا التعلم إذا قصد منه العلم دون العمل، والأول المختار، وجاء عن الصحابة أن يقتل الساحر، كذا ذكره الشيخ عبد الحق الدهلوي (وأكل مال اليتيم والعقوق أي عقوق أحد الوالدين أو كليهما ما لم يكن لأمر شرعي والإلحاد، أي الظلم في الحرم تخصيص الحرم لأن هتك حرمته أشد وزاد أبو هريرة أكل الربا و زاد أمير المؤمنين (علي) رضي الله عنه (السرقة وشرب الخمر نقل عن السبكي أن عد السرقة لم يثبت عنه بإسناد، وأما شرب الخمر فقد روي عنه شارب الخمر كعابد وثن (وقد زيد اليمين الغموس وهو اليمين على أمر ماض مع العلم بأنه كاذب فيه، وقد ثبت عده من الكبائر في الحديث الصحيح والإصرار على الصغائر) قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : لا كبيرة مع التوبة ولا صغيرة مع الإصرار والقمار والطعن في الصحابة والسلف الصالح وأراد به الإظهار بالسب فإنه مردود شهادة ورواية، وعلى هذا فالمراد بالمبتدع المتقدم من لم يظهر السب، وأما على التحقيق فالساب مطلقاً مردود رواية والسعي بالفساد) فإنه ذنب عظيم بالنص القرآني (وعدول الحاكم عن الحق بأن امتنع عن الحكم بالحق، سواء حكم بخلافه أو لم يحكم بشيء، والأول أشد من الثاني وقد شهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم بكون القاضيين الحاكم على خلاف الحق، والمتوقف عن الحكم بالحق في النار، قال الشيخ عبد الحق الدهلوي : اضطربت الأقوال في حد الكبائر وتعيينها فقيل : المذكورات هي الكبائر، وما دونها صغائر والمختار أنه ليس المراد الحصر، وقد روي عن ابن عباس أنه قال : الكبائر إلى سبعمائة أو ما قرب منها، بل النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أخبر في كل مجلس ما أوحي إليه، وما كان مفسدته مثل مفسدة شيء من المذكورات أو أكبر منها أيضاً من : فهو الكبائر، وإليه أشار المصنف بقوله (قيل : وكل ما مفسدته كأقل ما روي مفسدة) أو أزيد منه وهو ظاهر جداً ( فدلالة الكفار على المسلمين أكثر مفسدة (من الفرار) عن الزحف، فإن المفسدة فيه الهتك بكلمة الله تعالى والأول أشد فيه، وقال ذلك الشيخ، المثل فكشرب بعض المسكرات من غير الخمر وكاللواطة مثل الزنا، وكإيذاء الأستاذ مثل إيذاء الوالد وكالغصب مثل الربا، وأما الأكثر فمثل قطع الطريق مع أخذ المال أكثر من السرقة، وكذا إيذاء النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أكثر من إيذاء الولد، وكدلالة جيوش الكفار على بلاد المسلمين للغارة أكثر من الفرار عن الزحف وكحكم القاضي بغير الحق أكثر من شهادة الزور ظلماً ،وإثماً وقيل ما ثبت النهي عنه بنص قطعي، وقيل: ما قرن به في الشرع حد أو لعن أو وعيد وإلى هذا مال أكثرهم، وإليه أشار بقوله (وقيل: الكبيرة ما توعد عليه بخصوصه) وقال ذلك الشيخ، وعمم بعضهم هذا القول أيضاً وقال : وما مفسدته كمفسدة
As for the major sins (kaba'ir): according to Ibn 'Umar: polytheism with Allah, intentional murder without right, false accusation of a chaste woman, zina, fleeing from the battlefield (i.e., from facing the enemy disbelievers when the Muslims are not less than half), sorcery (i.e., learning and practicing it; some permitted learning for knowledge without practice, but the first is chosen), it is related from the Companions that the sorcerer is killed, as mentioned by Shaykh 'Abd al-Haqq al-Dihlawi, consuming the orphan's wealth, disobedience (to one or both parents unless for a legal reason), ilhad (injustice in the sanctuary, specifically because violating its sanctity is more severe). Abu Hurayra added: consuming riba. Amir al-Mu'minin 'Ali added: theft and drinking alcohol. It is reported from al-Subki that counting theft is not established by narration from him. As for drinking alcohol, it is reported from him: the drinker of wine is like a worshipper of an idol. Added: al-yamin al-ghamus (a false oath on a past matter knowingly), its being counted among major sins is established in a sound hadith. And persistence (israr) in minor sins. The Messenger said: There is no major sin with repentance, and no minor sin with persistence. Gambling, reviling the Companions and the righteous predecessors, meaning openly insulting, for such a person is rejected in testimony and narration. Based on this, the innovator mentioned earlier is one who does not openly revile. But as a matter of fact, the reviler is absolutely rejected in narration. And spreading corruption is a great sin by Quranic text. And the judge’s deviation from truth, by refusing to judge with truth, whether he judges contrary to it or judges nothing; the first is more severe. The Messenger testified that the two judges, the one who judges against truth and the one who refrains from judging with truth, are in the Fire. Shaykh 'Abd al-Haqq al-Dihlawi said: The statements about the definition and specification of major sins have varied. It is said: the mentioned ones are the major sins, and what is below them are minor sins. The chosen view is that enumeration is not intended for limitation. It is narrated from Ibn 'Abbas that he said: Major sins are up to seven hundred or close. Rather, the Prophet informed in every gathering of what was revealed to him. And whatever has corruption equal to or greater than any of the mentioned things is also from the major sins. To this the author pointed by his saying: It is said: everything whose corruption is like the least of what has been reported in corruption, or more. This is very apparent. So guiding the disbelievers to the Muslims has greater corruption than fleeing from battle, for the corruption in fleeing is the degradation of the Word of Allah, and the first is more severe in that. The shaykh gave examples: like drinking some intoxicants other than khamr, and like liwat (sodomy) is like zina, and like hurting a teacher is like hurting a parent, and like ghasp (usurpation) is like riba. As for greater: like highway robbery with taking money is greater than theft, likewise hurting the Prophet is greater than hurting a child, and guiding armies of disbelievers to Muslim lands for attack is greater than fleeing from battle, and the judge’s judgment without truth is greater than false testimony in oppression and sin. And it is said: [a major sin is] what is prohibited by a decisive text. And it is said: what in the Law is connected to a prescribed punishment (hadd) or curse (la'n) or threat (wa'id). The majority lean towards this, and to it the author pointed by saying: It is said: a major sin is that which is specifically threatened. The shaykh said that, and some generalized this view too and said: and whatever its corruption is like the corruption of... (text cuts off).
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage provides an extensive treatment of major sins (kaba'ir) in Hanafi usul. It begins with an enumeration attributed to several Companions: Ibn 'Umar lists shirk, murder, false accusation, zina, desertion, sorcery, consuming orphan's wealth, disobedience to parents, and injustice in the Haram. Abu Hurayra adds riba, and 'Ali adds theft and drinking alcohol, though the chain for theft is questioned. Other additions include the false oath (yamin ghamus), persistence in minor sins, gambling, reviling the Companions, spreading corruption, and judges deviating from truth. The passage then shifts to the theoretical definition of a major sin, quoting Shaykh 'Abd al-Haqq al-Dihlawi that opinions vary. The preferred view is that the list is not exhaustive; any act with similar or greater mafsada (corruption) than the named ones is also a major sin. The author (of Musallam al-Thubut) indicates two criteria: (1) what is prohibited by a decisive text (nass qat'i), and (2) what carries a prescribed punishment, curse, or specific threat in the Law. The majority favor the latter. Examples illustrate how acts like guiding enemies to Muslims or false judgment have greater mafsada than some listed sins. This establishes that the concept of kabira is relative to the magnitude of harm.

Key Terms
كبيرة (kabīrah): A major sin; acts with mafsada equal to or greater than those specifically mentioned as kabā'ir, or acts prohibited by decisive text or carrying a hadd/curse/threat.
صغيرة (ṣaghīrah): A minor sin; sins less than major sins, but persistence in them makes them major.
إصرار (iṣrār): Persistence in a minor sin without repentance, which elevates it to the level of a major sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the passage, which of the following is true about major sins?
A) They are limited to the seven enumerated by Ibn 'Umar.
B) Their definition includes acts with mafsada equal to or greater than the mentioned ones.
C) They are only those acts that carry a hadd punishment.
D) They are only those explicitly prohibited by a decisive text.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a major sin is not limited to a fixed list; it is defined by mafsada equivalence, decisive prohibition, or association with a hadd/curse/threat.

Defining Major Sins: Criteria and Impact on Narrator Integrity
Defines major and minor sins, their criteria, and impact on narrator reliability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi criteria for classifying a sin as major (kabirah) and apply them to evaluate narrator reliability.

Core Idea
A major sin is defined by odiousness, violation of religious sanctity, and corruption equivalent to or greater than specific examples.

In evaluating the integrity of a narrator (rawi), one key condition is abstention from major sins and base acts. This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahmut' provides a comprehensive analysis of what constitutes a major sin, moving beyond a fixed list to a principle based on the magnitude of corruption and societal outrage. Understanding this principle is vital for correctly assessing the reliability of transmitters.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the context
Note that the discussion occurs within conditions for narrator integrity: constant taqwa by avoiding major sins and base acts is the condition, not merely a stable trait (malaka).
This sets the stage for why defining major sins matters.
2
Catalog the major sins from Companions
Identify each sin listed from Ibn Umar, Abu Huraira, Ali, and additional ones like false oath and persistence on minor sins.
The list is not exhaustive; it provides a baseline for corruption equivalence.
3
Understand the criteria
The passage presents multiple definitions: corruption equivalence, specific threat (wa'id), hadd/curse/threat, odiousness with violation of religious sanctity.
The preferred definition is the last: what is odious among Muslims and involves violation of the sanctity of religion.
4
Compare and contrast definitions
Evaluate each definition: the relative view is rejected; the most correct is the corruption/odiousness criterion.
Note that the majority opinion (hadd/curse/threat) is also valid but narrower.
5
Apply to marginal cases
Consider acts like harming a teacher (analogized to harming parents) or lowly crafts (violating mura'a). Use the corruption equivalence and odiousness criteria to classify them.
This demonstrates how the definition is applied beyond the fixed list.

لا تنافي إتيان كبيرة مرة فالملازمة على التقوى بترك الكبائر والأفعال الخسيسة هي الشرط، لا أن الشرط حقيقة الملكة، وهذا مظنتها كما في المشقة والسفر فافهم. أما الكبائر فعن ابن عمر الشرك بالله تعالى والقتل عمداً من غير حق. وقذف المحصنة، والزنا والفرار من الزحف أي من مقابلة العدو الكافر الحربي لكن إذا لم يكن المسلمون أقل من النصف والسحر أي تعلمه والعمل به وبعضهم أباحوا التعلم إذا قصد منه العلم دون العمل، الحاكم على خلاف الحق، والمتوقف عن الحكم بالحق في النار، قال الشيخ عبد الحق الدهلوي : اضطربت الأقوال في حد الكبائر وتعيينها فقيل : المذكورات هي الكبائر، وما دونها صغائر والمختار أنه ليس المراد الحصر، وقد روي عن ابن عباس أنه قال : الكبائر إلى سبعمائة أو ما قرب منها، بل النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أخبر في كل مجلس ما أوحي إليه، وما كان مفسدته مثل مفسدة شيء من المذكورات أو أكبر منها أيضاً من : فهو الكبائر، وإليه أشار المصنف بقوله (قيل : وكل ما مفسدته كأقل ما روي مفسدة) أو أزيد منه وهو ظاهر جداً ( فدلالة الكفار على المسلمين أكثر مفسدة (من الفرار) عن الزحف، فإن المفسدة فيه الهتك بكلمة الله تعالى والأول أشد فيه، وقال ذلك الشيخ، المثل فكشرب بعض المسكرات من غير الخمر وكاللواطة مثل الزنا، وكإيذاء الأستاذ مثل إيذاء الوالد وكالغصب مثل الربا، وأما الأكثر فمثل قطع الطريق مع أخذ المال أكثر من السرقة، وكذا إيذاء النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أكثر من إيذاء الولد، وكدلالة جيوش الكفار على بلاد المسلمين للغارة أكثر من الفرار عن الزحف وكحكم القاضي بغير الحق أكثر من شهادة الزور ظلماً ،وإثماً وقيل ما ثبت النهي عنه بنص قطعي، وقيل: ما قرن به في الشرع حد أو لعن أو وعيد وإلى هذا مال أكثرهم، وإليه أشار بقوله (وقيل: الكبيرة ما توعد عليه بخصوصه) وقال ذلك الشيخ، وعمم بعضهم هذا القول أيضاً وقال : وما مفسدته كمفسدة ما قرن به أحد الثلاثة وأكثر ، وقيل : ما أشعر بتهاون المرتكب بالدين إشعاراً مثل إشعار الكبائر، كقتل رجل يعتقد أنه معصوم الدم فظهر أنه مستحق له، وكوطء زوجته وهو يظنها أجنبية، ونقل عن «الكافي»، والأصح ما كان شنيعاً بين المسلمين وفيه هتك حرمة الدين، فهو كبيرة، وإلا فهو صغيرة وأما ما قيل : كل معصية أصر عليها العبد فهي كبيرة، وكل ما استغفر عنها فهي صغيرة فيلزم منه أن يكون الزنا والقتل والشرب صغائر، إذ لم يصر عليها، اللهم إلا أن يريد ما عدا المنصوصة وأغرب منه ما نقل عن صاحب «الكفاية» وقال: الحق أنهما أمران إضافيان لا يفترقان بذاتيهما، فكل معصية أضيفت إلى ما فوقها فهي صغيرة، وإن اضيفت إلى ما تحتها فهي كبيرة وهذا مشكل جداً، فإن الكبائر والصغائر متمايزتان بالذات وبالأحكام، فإن الصغائر تكفرها الطاعات مثل الصلاة والصوم والوضوء وعليه يحمل قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْحَسَنَتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ ﴾ :[ هود : ١١٤] انتهى كلامه وما يخل بالمروءة منها صغائر دالة على خسة، كسرقة لقمة واشتراط الأجرة على الحديث عليه الإمام أحمد رحمه الله تعالى، ونقل عن وكيع : لا بأس به، قال ابن الصلاح مثله مثل الأجرة على تعليم القرآن، إلا أن يخل بالمروءة إن لم يكن عن عذر (و) منها (مباحات مثلها كالأكل والبول في الطريق) : وقيل في إباحة البول في الطريق نظر لورود النهي عنه والحرف الدنية كالحياكة والصياغة وفي ( التيسير في بعض فروع الشافعية أن اعياد الحياكة وهي حرفة أبيه فلا وفي «الروضة» ينبغي لا يقيد به وينظر إلى أنه يليق به أم لا ولبس الفقيه قباء فإنه عادة المسرفين
Committing a major sin once does not negate Taqwa? Actually the constant Taqwa by abandoning major sins and base acts is the condition, not that the condition is the actual state (malakah), and this is its locus as in hardship and travel, so understand. As for major sins: from Ibn Umar: Shirk with Allah, intentional murder without right, slander of chaste women, adultery, fleeing from battle i.e. from facing the disbelieving enemy but if Muslims are not less than half, magic i.e. learning and acting by it, and some permitted learning if intended for knowledge and not action, but the first is chosen; it is narrated from the Companions that the sorcerer is killed, as mentioned by Sheikh Abd al-Haqq al-Dihlawi; consuming orphan's wealth, disobedience i.e. to one or both parents unless for a lawful matter; heresy i.e. injustice in the sanctuary, specifying the sanctuary because violating its sanctity is more severe; Abu Huraira added: consuming usury; and Amir al-Mu'minin Ali added: theft and drinking wine. It is reported from al-Subki that counting theft from him is not established with a chain, but as for drinking wine, it is reported from him: the wine drinker is like an idol worshipper. And added: the oath of falsehood (al-yamin al-ghamus) i.e. swearing about a past matter while knowing one is lying, and its counting among major sins is established in the authentic hadith; and persistence on minor sins: the Messenger of Allah said: 'No major sin with repentance, no minor sin with persistence'; gambling; slandering the Companions and the righteous predecessors, meaning by explicit cursing, for such a person is rejected in testimony and narration; on this basis, the innovator referred to earlier is one who does not manifest cursing, but on investigation, the curser absolutely is rejected in narration; spreading corruption, for it is a great sin by Quranic text; the ruler deviating from truth by refraining from judging with truth, whether he judges contrary to it or does not judge at all, the first is worse; the Messenger of Allah testified that the two judges—the one judging contrary to truth and the one refraining from judging with truth—are in Fire. Sheikh Abd al-Haqq al-Dihlawi said: Statements have varied on the definition of major sins and their specification: it was said the mentioned ones are the major sins, and what is below them are minor; the chosen view is that enumeration is not intended; Ibn Abbas said major sins number up to seven hundred or so; the Prophet informed in every gathering what was revealed to him, and whatever has corruption similar to the corruption of the mentioned ones or greater is also a major sin; the author indicated with his saying: 'it was said: every act whose corruption is like the least of what has been narrated in corruption or more' which is very apparent. So guiding disbelievers to Muslims is greater in corruption than fleeing from battle, for the corruption therein is disgrace to the word of Allah, and the first is more severe in it; and the example said: drinking some intoxicants other than wine is like adultery, sodomy is like adultery, harming a teacher is like harming a parent, usurpation is like usury; as for greater: highway robbery with taking wealth is greater than theft, harming the Prophet is greater than harming a child, guiding armies of disbelievers to Muslim lands for attack is greater than fleeing from battle, and a judge judging falsely is greater than false testimony in oppression and sin. And it was said: what is prohibited by a definitive text; and it was said: what in law is accompanied by a prescribed punishment, curse, or threat, and this is the opinion of most; the author indicated with: 'it was said: a major sin is what is specifically threatened', and that sheikh said so; some generalized this statement saying: and what has corruption like the corruption of what is accompanied by one of the three or more; and it was said: what indicates the perpetrator's carelessness with religion in a way similar to major sins, like killing a man one believes is protected but turns out to be deserving, or having intercourse with one's wife thinking she is a stranger; it is transmitted from al-Kafi; the most correct is: what is odious among Muslims and involves violating the sanctity of religion is a major sin, otherwise it is minor. As for what was said: every sin the servant persists in is major, and every sin he seeks forgiveness for is minor, then it would follow that adultery, murder, and drinking are minor if not persisted in, unless he intends other than the prescribed ones. And stranger is what was transmitted from the author of al-Kifayah: the correct view is that they are relative concepts not distinct in themselves, every sin added to a higher one becomes minor, and added to a lower one becomes major. This is very problematic, for major and minor are distinct in essence and rulings; minor sins are expiated by acts of obedience like prayer, fasting, and ablution, and on this is based the verse: 'Indeed good deeds do away with misdeeds' (Hud 11:114). End of his speech. And what violates mura'a from them are minor sins indicating baseness, like stealing a morsel and stipulating a fee for hadith transmission; Imam Ahmad disapproved of the latter, Wakil said no harm, Ibn al-Salah said it is like a fee for teaching Quran, unless it violates mura'a if not for an excuse; and from them are permissible acts like eating and urinating in the road, though there is consideration regarding the permissibility of urinating in the road due to the prohibition; and lowly crafts like weaving and jewelry-making. In al-Taysir on some Shafi'i branches, returning to weaving which is his father's craft is not recommended; in al-Rawda, it should not be restricted, but considered whether it befits him or not; and a faqih wearing a robe is the habit of the extravagant.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of major sins (kaba'ir) and minor sins (sagha'ir) as they pertain to the condition a narrator (rawi) must meet for his testimony and narration to be accepted. The author begins by emphasizing that the constant practice of taqwa by avoiding major sins and base acts is the condition for reliability, not merely having a state of malakah (stable trait). He then lists specifically transmitted major sins from various companions: shirk, murder, slander of chaste women, adultery, fleeing from battle, magic, consuming orphan's wealth, disobedience to parents, heresy in the sanctuary, and adds from Abu Huraira and Ali (theft, wine, false oath). Persistence on minor sins is also counted as major. The author then moves to definitions: it is said any act whose level of corruption is equal to or greater than the mentioned ones is major; another definition is that a major sin is specifically threatened with punishment in the hereafter (وعيد), or in the world with a hadd penalty or curse. The most correct definition is that a major sin is what is odious among Muslims and involves violation of the sanctity of religion. The author refutes the view that major/minor are relative by arguing that sins have intrinsic differences: minor sins are expiated by good deeds like prayer, as per Quran 11:114. Finally, acts that violate mura'a (decency) are classified as minor sins, with examples: stealing a morsel, charging a fee for hadith transmission (disputed if it violates mura'a), urinating in the road (makruh due to prohibition), and engaging in low crafts like weaving or jewelry-making, or wearing a robe that resembles the extravagant.

Key Terms
الكبيرة (al-kabirah): What is odious among Muslims and involves violation of the sanctity of religion, or what is accompanied by hadd, curse, or specific threat; corruption equivalent to or greater than listed sins.
المروءة (al-mura'a): Decency and proper conduct; acts violating mura'a are minor sins indicating baseness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is explicitly added by Abu Huraira to the list of major sins?
A) Magic
B) Usury (riba)
C) Theft
D) Slander of chaste women

Key Takeaway
A major sin is defined not by a fixed list but by the magnitude of corruption, societal odiousness, and violation of religious sanctity; this principle is essential for evaluating narrator integrity in Hanafi Usul.

Distinguishing Major and Minor Sins: Hanafi Usul Definition
Definition and defense of kabirah vs saghirah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of a major sin (kabirah) and apply it to distinguish between major and minor sins, critiquing the relativist position.

Core Idea
Major sins are heinous acts violating religious sanctity; minor sins are expiated by good deeds.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut tackles a fundamental usul question: how do we define a 'major sin'? The Hanafi school insists that the distinction between major and minor sins is not relative but essential, based on the nature of the act. Understanding this classification has practical implications for expiation and legal testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi definition of kabirah
Locate in the source passage the phrase 'ما كان شنيعاً بين المسلمين وفيه هتك حرمة الدين' — a sin heinous among Muslims involving violation of religious sanctity. This is the approved definition.
This definition is from al-Kafi.
2
Understand the criticism of the persistence-based view
The passage critiques the idea that persistence determines a sin's gravity. It gives counterexamples: fornication and murder are major even if not persisted in.
The critique uses reductio ad absurdum.
3
Assess the relativist argument from al-Kifayah
The relativist view claims that major/minor are relative to comparison. The passage rejects this as 'very problematic' and affirms essential distinction.
Hanafi position: the categories are fixed by shari'ah.
4
Apply the Quranic evidence
The passage cites Quran 11:114 to show that minor sins are expiated by good deeds, while major sins require specific repentance.
This textual proof supports the essential distinction.
5
Connect to mura'a
The passage discusses acts that detract from mura'a as minor sins, linking the conceptual definition to practical Hanafi rulings on witness credibility.
Al-mura'a is important in Hanafi fiqh for tazkiyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن ما قرن به أحد الثلاثة وأكثر ، وقيل : ما أشعر بتهاون المرتكب بالدين إشعاراً مثل إشعار الكبائر، كقتل رجل يعتقد أنه معصوم الدم فظهر أنه مستحق له، وكوطء زوجته وهو يظنها أجنبية، ونقل عن «الكافي»، والأصح ما كان شنيعاً بين المسلمين وفيه هتك حرمة الدين، فهو كبيرة، وإلا فهو صغيرة وأما ما قيل : كل معصية أصر عليها العبد فهي كبيرة، وكل ما استغفر عنها فهي صغيرة فيلزم منه أن يكون الزنا والقتل والشرب صغائر، إذ لم يصر عليها، اللهم إلا أن يريد ما عدا المنصوصة وأغرب منه ما نقل عن صاحب «الكفاية» وقال: الحق أنهما أمران إضافيان لا يفترقان بذاتيهما، فكل معصية أضيفت إلى ما فوقها فهي صغيرة، وإن اضيفت إلى ما تحتها فهي كبيرة وهذا مشكل جداً، فإن الكبائر والصغائر متمايزتان بالذات وبالأحكام، فإن الصغائر تكفرها الطاعات مثل الصلاة والصوم والوضوء وعليه يحمل قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْحَسَنَتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ ﴾ :[ هود : ١١٤] انتهى كلامه وما يخل بالمروءة منها صغائر دالة على خسة، كسرقة لقمة واشتراط الأجرة على الحديث عليه الإمام أحمد رحمه الله تعالى، ونقل عن وكيع : لا بأس به، قال ابن الصلاح مثله مثل الأجرة على تعليم القرآن، إلا أن يخل بالمروءة إن لم يكن عن عذر (و) منها (مباحات مثلها كالأكل والبول في الطريق) : وقيل في إباحة البول في الطريق نظر لورود النهي عنه والحرف الدنية كالحياكة والصياغة وفي ( التيسير في بعض فروع الشافعية أن اعياد الحياكة وهي حرفة أبيه فلا وفي «الروضة» ينبغي لا يقيد به وينظر إلى أنه يليق به أم لا ولبس الفقيه قباء فإنه عادة المسرفين
That which is combined with one of the three and more, and it is said: that which indicates the perpetrator's disregard for religion in a manner similar to the indication of major sins, such as killing a man whom he believed was inviolable in blood but turned out to be deserving of it, and such as intercourse with his wife while thinking she was a stranger. It is transmitted from al-Kafi: the correct position is that whatever is heinous among Muslims and involves violating the sanctity of religion is a major sin, otherwise it is a minor sin. As for what is said: every disobedience that the servant persists in is major, and every one for which he seeks forgiveness is minor, that would entail that fornication, murder, and drinking are minor sins, since one does not persist in them. Unless what is meant is other than the specified sins. More strange is what is transmitted from the author of al-Kifayah: he said the truth is that they are two relative matters, not distinct in themselves; every sin added to something above it is minor, and if added to something below it is major. This is very problematic, for major and minor sins are distinct in essence and in rulings. Minor sins are expiated by acts of obedience such as prayer, fasting, and ablution, and upon this is borne His statement: "Indeed, good deeds remove evil deeds" (Hud 114). End of his words. What detracts from manliness (mura'a) among them are minor sins indicating baseness, such as stealing a morsel and requiring a fee for narrating hadith. Imam Ahmad (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated regarding this, and it is transmitted from Waki': there is no harm in it. Ibn al-Salah said its example is like the fee for teaching the Quran, except if it detracts from manliness unless there is an excuse. And among them are permissible acts like eating and urinating in the street. It is said regarding the permissibility of urinating in the street that there is doubt due to the prohibition reported. And lowly professions such as weaving and dyeing. In al-Taysir concerning some branches of Shafi'i jurisprudence: renewing the profession of weaving, and it is his father's profession, so no. And in al-Rawdah: it should not be restricted thereby, and one should consider whether it suits him or not. And the scholar's wearing a jubba is the habit of the extravagant.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the definition and classification of major and minor sins (kabirah and saghirah) within Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author presents multiple views. The first view: a major sin is one that the perpetrator disregards religion with an indication similar to known major sins, giving examples of a man killing someone he wrongly thought was lawfully targetable, or having intercourse with his wife thinking she was a stranger. The correct view according to al-Kafi is that a sin is major if it is heinous among Muslims and violates religious sanctity; otherwise it is minor. The author then critiques the view that persistence makes a sin major, showing that it would absurdly make fornication and murder minor. He also strongly rejects the relativist position from al-Kifayah, arguing that major and minor sins are distinct in essence and rulings, as minor sins are expiated by good deeds (citing Quran 11:114). The passage then turns to acts that detract from mura'a (manliness or decency), which are considered minor sins indicating baseness, like stealing a small morsel or charging a fee for teaching hadith. It discusses the ruling on such fees, citing Imam Ahmad permitting only if not detracting from mura'a. Permissible acts like eating and urinating in public are included, with a note of doubt on urination due to prohibition. Lowly trades like weaving are mentioned, with a Shafi'i note that a weaver should not be prohibited if it is his family trade, and a Hanbali note that the scholar's wearing a distinct garment (qaba') is like extravagant ones.

Key Terms
كبيرة (kabīrah): A sin that is heinous among Muslims and involves violating the sanctity of religion; requires specific repentance.
صغيرة (saghīrah): A sin that does not reach the level of heinousness; may be expiated by good deeds.
المروءة (al-murū'ah): Manliness or decency; behaviour that is socially appropriate for a person of status. Acts that detract from it are considered minor sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view approved in this passage, which of the following is a major sin?
A) Stealing a small morsel of food
B) Killing a person one wrongly thinks is legally targetable
C) Intercourse with one's wife under a false belief that she is a stranger
D) Charging a fee for teaching hadith

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul defines major sins by heinousness and violation of religious sanctity, distinct from minor sins which are expiated by good deeds.

The Limits of Istishab: Presumption of 'Adala and the Need for Evidence
Discusses the presumption of Islam and 'adala via istishab and its limits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that istishab is valid only for defense (daf'), not for establishing entitlement (istihqaq), and identify when a countervailing factor (like the overwhelming of desire) prevents its application.

Core Idea
Istishab suffices for defense, not for entitlement—presumption cannot override the opposing force of desire.

This lesson examines a key limitation of the usul principle of istishab as articulated in Fawatih al-Rahamut. The passage uses the example of a person born in Dar al-Islam to illustrate that while the default is Islam and purity, this presumption cannot automatically establish the 'adala (moral probity) required for accepting testimony or narration. The text argues that istishab is only a proof for defense, not for establishing rights, and that the natural inclination of desire (hawa) opposes the presumption of continued 'adala. Understanding this limitation is essential for correctly applying istishab in rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the presumption
The passage discusses a person born in Dar al-Islam with unknown traces of faith or disbelief. The default regarding their Islam is established by the fitra and the principle that what was remains as it was.
This is the starting point for applying istishab.
2
Determine the function of istishab
The author states that istishab is not a proof (hujja) for establishing acceptance of speech (i.e., proving 'adala for testimony or narration). It only suffices for defense (daf') not for entitlement (istihqaq).
Hanafis limit istishab to ward off claims, not to assert new ones.
3
Identify the countervailing factor
Although 'adala is originally presumed, the constant overwhelming of desire (ghalabat al-hawa) in humans serves as a countervailing factor that opposes the presumption.
Without evidence that the person has overcome desire, the presumption weakens.
4
Conclude the lesson's point
The passage concludes that there is no basis for the continuation of 'adala unless there is evidence contradicting desire. Thus, istishab alone is insufficient to establish 'adala.
This teaches the importance of evidence (like tazkiya) over mere presumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثاني/ السنة. بمن لم يعلم منه آثار الكفر والإيمان لكن ولد في دار الإسلام فإن المقدمات جارية فيه، فإن ولادته على الفطرة فطرة الإسلام والإسلام على الطهارة، والأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان، وثانياً : هذا الاستدلال بالاستصحاب وهو غير حجة، وإنما تكفي الأصالة للدفع عند بعض المشايخ لا للاستحقاق، فلا يصلح حجة في إثبات قبول القول وثالثاً : أصالة بقاء ما كان على ما كان إنما هي إذا لم يعارضها معارض وههنا العدالة وإن كانت أصلاً لكن ملازمة غلبة الهوى على الإنسان تعارضها فلا وجه لبقائها ما لم يدل دليل على مخالفة الهوى فافهم وتثبت .
The second source: the Sunnah. Regarding one whose traces of disbelief and faith are unknown but was born in Dar al-Islam, the premises apply: his birth is upon the fitra of Islam and Islam is upon purity, and the original is that what was remains as it was. Secondly, this inference by istishab is not a proof; the original presumption suffices for defense according to some mashayikh, not for entitlement, so it is not valid as proof for establishing acceptance of speech. Thirdly, the original preservation of what was is only valid when there is no opposing factor; here, although 'adala is an original, the constant overwhelming of desire opposes it, so there is no basis for its continuation unless there is evidence contradicting desire. So understand and be certain.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage discusses the limitation of the legal principle of istishab (presumption of continuity) in establishing the 'adala of an individual. It begins with the case of a person born in Dar al-Islam without known signs of faith or disbelief: the default is that they are Muslim (fitra) and pure. However, when trying to use this presumption to prove acceptance of their testimony or narration, it is argued that istishab is not a valid proof for such entitlement; it only suffices for defense (daf'). Moreover, the presumption of continuity of 'adala is opposed by the natural tendency of humans towards desire (hawa), which necessitates evidence to the contrary. The lesson underscores that 'adala cannot be assumed merely by default; it requires actual evidence.

Key Terms
الاستصحاب (al-istishab): Presumption of continuity of a previously established state until evidence of change.
الأصالة (al-asala): The original presumption or default ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is istishab insufficient to establish 'adala?
A) Because the person was not born Muslim.
B) Because istishab is only a proof for defense, not entitlement.
C) Because 'adala is not an original state.
D) Because the person might be a liar.

Key Takeaway
Istishab cannot establish 'adala because it only serves defense and is opposed by the inherent tendency of desire.

Default Ruling of the Prophet's Actions: Ibahah as the Hanafi Position
Arguing for ibahah as the default for the Prophet's actions without qurbah indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position that the default ruling of the Prophet's actions when no qurbah is apparent is ibahah, and evaluate the opposing view that it is mandub.

Core Idea
When no intention of nearness is apparent, the Prophet's action is mubah — not automatically mandub.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut tackles a key usul question: what is the default ruling of the Prophet's actions when we don't know if he intended them as acts of worship? The answer determines whether followers are rewarded for emulating every action or only those with clear devotional intent. Let's follow the Hanafi argument for ibahah and the counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
State the two main positions: some scholars argue the default is mandub (recommended) because the Prophet was mostly engaged in acts of qurbah; the majority of Hanafis argue the default is ibahah (permissible) when no qurbah is apparent. Also note the suspension (tawaqquf) position.
2
Trace the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi evidence: if the action were not permitted, it would have to be either haram or specific to the Prophet. Both are negated by lack of proof of sin or exclusivity.
This is a talil (rational proof) based on negation of alternatives.
3
Understand the counterargument and response
A counterargument says the reasoning only works for 'general permission' (permission even if leaving is difficult) not 'special permission' (equal permission to do and leave). The Hanafis respond they indeed negate the extra element of difficulty or reward, distinguishing from the advocates of suspension.
Special permission = ibahah; general permission could imply mandub.
4
Evaluate the suspension argument
Those who suspend judgment (wāqifīyah) argue that the action could be specific or general, so no ruling can be assigned. The Hanafi response: prophethood is for following, so a specific ruling must be determined by each mujtahid based on his evidence.
This leads to the conclusion that all positions are valid if supported by evidence.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: if mandub, emulating the action brings reward; if mubah, no reward nor blame. This affects daily practice of the sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مندوب وما هو مباح لم يمدح عليه (و) قالوا : (ثانياً : الغالب في أفعاله الندب) فيحمل عليه ويكون مندوباً أيضاً، للتأسي (وأجيب) لا نسلم غلبة المندوب (بل) الغالب (المباح أقول في غير الجبلية من الأفعال الظاهر غيره لأن جل همته صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم الاشتغال بالقرب وجوابه أن عند عدم ظهور القربة الغالب المباح كما يظهر لمن تتبع أحواله الشريفة حرصاً على التسهيل على الأمة . أكثر الحنفية قالوا : الإباحة هو المتيقن عند عدم قرينة القربة لأنه لو لم يكن مأذوناً فيه لكان حراماً عليه وعلينا أو مختصاً به وهما منتفيان الانتفاء المعصية والخصوصية) وأقل مراتب المأذون الإباحة لعدم الأمر الزائد المفروض إنه لا قربة، فلا وجوب ولا ندب وذلك لأن الأحكام فيما لم يظهر قصد القربة، فلو كان لبينه لكثرة الاهتمام بالقرب فتعين الإباحة، وعلى هذا اندفع ما قال قد يقال : إنما يتم هذا الوجه (لو كان المدعي الإمكان العام وهو مطلق الإذن أعم من أن يكون مع الحرج في الترك أم لا (لا الإمكان الخاص وهو الإذن في الفعل مع الإذن في الترك، لكن مدعاهم هو الثاني كيف لا ( وقد نفوا الزائد) على الإذن من الحرج في الترك أو استحقاق المثوبة بالفعل مع عدم الحرج في الترك امتيازاً عن الواقفية ولو لم ينفوا لكان قولهم بالتوقف (اللهم إلا أن يثبتوا جواز الترك (بالأصل فافهم المتوقفون (قالوا الفعل ( يحتمل الخصوص) بالنبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (والعموم) للأمة منتهياً إلى أنواع شتى من الوجوب والندب والإباحة (فالحكم) بأحدها على الأمة (تحكم) فوجب التوقف (الجواب) الاحتمالات وإن كانت كثيرة لكن وضع النبوة للاقتداء وهو دليل الحكم فلا بد من حكم معين على مشارب مختلفة) فمن يرى الوجوب فعنده دليل الوجوب ومن يرى الندب فعنده دليل الندب ومن يرى الإباحة فعنده دليل الإباحة وهو الصحيح (فتدبر).
(Commentary on the statement: 'Recommended and what is permissible is not praised' and they said: 'Secondly, the majority of his actions are recommended' so it is carried upon that and is also recommended for emulation. Response: We do not concede the predominance of the recommended. Rather, the predominant is the permissible. I say regarding non-natural actions the apparent is other than that because most of his concern, peace be upon him and his family and companions, was devotion to acts of nearness. The answer to that is that when no manifestation of nearness appears, the predominant is permissibility, as appears to one who follows his noble state out of eagerness to facilitate for the nation. The majority of the Hanafis said: Permissibility is the certain ruling when there is no indication of nearness, because if it were not permitted, it would be forbidden for him and for us, or specific to him, and both are negated (negation of disobedience and specificity). And the lowest rank of what is permitted is ibahah due to the lack of additional command, assuming there is no nearness, so no obligation and no recommendation. And this is because rulings concerning actions where no intention of nearness is apparent: if there were nearness, he would have explained it due to his great concern for nearness; so permissibility is determined. And upon this is refuted what might be said: This argument is only valid if one claims general permission (absolute permission whether with difficulty in leaving or not), not special permission (permission to do along with permission to leave). But their claim is the second, how could it not be, and they have negated the extra element beyond permission—difficulty in leaving or deserving reward for doing without difficulty in leaving—distinguishing from the advocates of suspension. If they had not negated, their statement would be suspension. Except that they establish the permissibility of leaving as the origin, so understand. Those who suspend judgment said: The action is likely specific to the Prophet or general to the nation extending to various types of obligation, recommendation, and permissibility. So judging one of them upon the nation is arbitrary, so suspension is necessary. Answer: The possibilities, though many, but the status of prophethood is for following, and that is the evidence of the ruling. So a specific ruling is necessary according to different approaches: whoever sees obligation has his evidence, whoever sees recommendation has his evidence, and whoever sees permissibility has his evidence, and that is correct. Reflect.)
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut discusses the default ruling of the Prophet's actions when there is no clear intention of qurbah (nearness to God). It begins by addressing the view that the majority of the Prophet's actions are mandub (recommended) and therefore every action should be considered recommended for emulation. The Hanafi response is that this is not accepted; rather, the majority of his actions, especially those not naturally ingrained, are mubah (permissible) when no qurbah is apparent. The evidence is that if the action were not permitted, it would have to be either haram for him and us, or specific to him. Both are negated: disobedience is negated because he is innocent, and specificity is negated because no proof of exclusivity exists. The least level of permission is ibahah, since there is no additional command to indicate obligation or recommendation. The text then addresses a counterargument that the reasoning only works if the claimed permission is 'general permission' (permission to do even if leaving is difficult) not 'special permission' (permission to do and permission to leave equally). The Hanafis respond that they indeed negate the extra element of difficulty in leaving or reward for doing, which distinguishes them from the advocates of suspension (wāqifīyah). The passage concludes with the statement of those who suspend judgment, who argue that the action could be specific to the Prophet or general, and among the general rulings there are many possibilities, so suspending judgment is necessary. The Hanafi response is that although there are many possibilities, the purpose of prophethood is for the ummah to follow, so a specific ruling must be determined by each mujtahid based on his evidence, and all are valid.

Key Terms
الإباحة (al-ibahah): Permissibility; the ruling that an action is allowed and not blameworthy or rewarded.
القربة (al-qurbah): An act that brings one closer to Allah; an intention of devotion.
الندب (al-nadb): Recommendation; an action that is rewarded but not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the default ruling of the Prophet's action when no intention of qurbah is apparent?
A) Wajib
B) Mandub
C) Mubah
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
When no indication of qurbah is present, the default ruling of the Prophet's action is ibahah (permissibility), not mandub.

Following the Companion in Matters Beyond Opinion
Ruling on following Companion in matters beyond human reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on taqlid of a Companion in ma la yudrak bi al-ra'y, apply it to the example of menstruation duration, and evaluate the objection raised against using the Companion's saying as evidence.

Core Idea
A Companion's saying in matters beyond opinion is binding as transmitted revelation.

This passage establishes a key usul principle: when a ruling cannot be determined by reason (like the minimum days of menstruation), the statement of a Companion becomes authoritative evidence. The Hanafis hold it obligatory to follow such statements, treating them as quasi-revelatory. The author defends this against an objection that a weak hadith might also exist, showing that multiple proofs converge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine what is meant by 'ma la yudrak bi al-ra'y' – matters that human reason cannot ascertain independently, such as specific numbers or rituals.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
According to the passage, taqlid of a Companion in such matters is wajib (obligatory) by agreement (ittifaqan).
3
Examine the first example
The estimation of minimum menstruation (three days) is given by multiple Companions (Ibn Mas'ud, Anas, etc.) – a case of taqdir not reachable by opinion.
The author cites this as evidence for the rule.
4
Address the objection
An imaginary objector points to a weak hadith from al-Darqutni. The response is that the hadith becomes hasan through multiple chains, so both the hadith and the Companion's saying support the same ruling.
The phrase ' لا بأس غاية الأمر أنه قام دليلان على مطلوب واحد' confirms the convergence.
5
Understand the second example
The story of 'A'ishah's ruling on a sale with a condition shows that the voiding of jihad cannot be by opinion – it must be based on hearing (samai'). Thus, 'A'ishah's report is treated as transmitted evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و تقليد الصحابي فيما لا يدرك بالرأي فعند أصحابنا) يجب اتفاقاً، كتقدير أقل الحيض بقول ابن مسعود وأنس وأمير المؤمنين عمر وأمير المؤمنين علي وعثمان وابن أبي العاص عزاه في التيسير» إلى «جامع الأسرار»، فإن التقديرات مما لا يهتدي إليه الرأي، فإن قلت : قد روى الدارقطني عنه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم مرفوعاً: «أقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام وأكثره عشرة أيام، فههنا عمل به لا بقول الصحابي. وهذا الحديث وإن كان في سنده ضعف لكن صار بتعدد الطرق حسناً قابلاً للاحتجاج كما بينه الشيخ ابن الهمام في فتح القدير»، قلت لا بأس به غاية الأمر أنه قام دليلان على مطلوب واحد مثال آخر: روى رزين عن أم أنس قالت: جاءت أم ولد زيد بن أرقم إلى أم المؤمنين عائشة فقالت : بعت جارية من زيد بثمانمائة درهم إلى العطاء، ثم اشتريتها قبل حلول الأجل بستمائة وكنت شرطت عليه إن بعتها فأنا اشتريها منك. فقالت لها عائشة : بئسما شريت وبئسما اشتريت أبلغي زيد بن أرقم أنه قد أبطل جهاده مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم إن لم يتب منه قالت فما نصنع؟ قال : قالت عائشة : فَمَن جَاءَهُ مَوْعِظَةٌ مِّن رَّبِّهِ فَانتَهَى ، : فَلَهُ مَا سَلَفَ وَأَمْرُهُ إِلَى الله ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٧٥] ومن عاد فينتقم الله منه والحكم ببطلان الجهاد لا يكون بالرأي، فلا بد من السماع
And taqlid of the Companion in what is not known by opinion – according to our scholars it is obligatory by agreement, like the estimation of the minimum menstruation by the saying of Ibn Mas'ud, Anas, 'Umar, 'Ali, 'Uthman, and Ibn Abi al-'As, as attributed in al-Taysir to Jami' al-Asrar. For such estimations are not reached by opinion. If you say: al-Darqutni narrated from him, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and Companions, as a marfu' hadith: 'The minimum menstruation is three days, the maximum ten days.' So here one acts on it, not on the Companion's saying. This hadith, although its chain has weakness, has become hasan by multiple paths and acceptable for argument, as Shaykh Ibn al-Humam explained in Fath al-Qadir. I say: there is no harm; the point is that two evidences stand for one desired matter. Another example: Razin narrated from Umm Anas who said: The mother of Zaid ibn Arqam's child came to 'A'ishah and said: I sold a slave girl from Zaid for eight hundred dirhams deferred until the stipend, then I bought her back before the term for six hundred, and I had stipulated that if he sold her, I would buy her from you. 'A'ishah said: What a bad sale and what a bad purchase! Inform Zaid ibn Arqam that his jihad with the Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and Companions, is void if he does not repent. She said: What shall we do? 'A'ishah said: 'Then whoever receives an admonition from his Lord and stops, he shall have what has passed, and his affair is left to Allah' [2:275], and whoever returns, Allah will take vengeance. And the ruling of voiding jihad cannot be by opinion, so it must be by hearing.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about following a Companion in matters that cannot be determined by personal opinion (ma la yudrak bi al-ra'y). According to the Hanafi school, it is obligatory by consensus to follow the Companion in such matters. Examples include the minimum duration of menstruation: estimates from Ibn Mas'ud, Anas, 'Umar, 'Ali, 'Uthman, and Ibn Abi al-'As are cited. The author then addresses an objection: there is a marfu' hadith from al-Darqutni giving the same estimation, so perhaps one acts on the hadith, not the Companion's saying. The response is that even if the hadith is weak, it becomes hasan through multiple chains, so there are two proofs (hadith and Companion saying) for the same ruling. Another example is from the practice of 'A'ishah regarding a sale with a condition (bay' al-'inah or a deferred sale with repurchase). She declared that such a transaction voids the jihad of Zaid ibn Arqam unless he repents, citing the Quranic verse on usury. This ruling (bona fide of jihad) cannot be based on mere opinion, so it must be based on hearing (samai') from the Prophet, which is transmitted through the Companion. Thus the Companion's saying is treated as evidence for rulings beyond human reason.

Key Terms
تقليد الصحابي (taqlid al-sahabi): Following the opinion of a Companion without questioning its evidence.
ما لا يدرك بالرأي (ma la yudrak bi al-ra'y): Matters that cannot be determined by human reasoning, such as specific quantities and rituals.
تقدير (taqdir): Estimation or determination of a specific measure, like days of menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on following a Companion in matters that cannot be known by personal opinion (ma la yudrak bi al-ra'y)?
A) It is recommended but not obligatory.
B) It is obligatory by agreement.
C) It is prohibited because it is taqlid.
D) It depends on whether the Companion is a Caliph.

Key Takeaway
In matters beyond human reasoning, a Companion's statement is binding evidence that must be followed.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Hujjiyah of the Sahabi's Opinion as a Transmitted Proof
Argues that the Sahabi's opinion is a hujjah and must be followed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers the opinion of a Sahabi in non-reasoned matters to be a binding proof (hujjah) equivalent to a marfu' hadith, and distinguish between the authority of a Sahabi and a Tabi'i in this regard.

Core Idea
A Sahabi's statement in matters beyond personal reasoning is a proof – it stands in place of a direct transmission from the Prophet.

This passage clarifies a distinctive Hanafi usul position: the opinion of a Companion (Sahabi) on matters that cannot be arrived at by independent reasoning is considered a transmitted proof, not mere ijtihad. Because acting without any legal proof is forbidden, and rational proof is excluded by assumption, the Sahabi's statement must be based on hearing from the Prophet. Hence it carries the weight of a marfu' hadith. The author defends this view against objections concerning why a Sahabi himself must follow another Sahabi, and why a Tabi'i (Successor) is not given the same status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The author states that the opinion (madhhab) of a Sahabi in matters where personal opinion has no scope (e.g., unseen matters, rulings transmitted from the Prophet) is in the ruling of a marfu' hadith – i.e., it is a transmitted proof (hujjah naqliyah).
‘Matters beyond opinion’ refers to issues like punishment of the grave, details of worship not deducible by reason.
2
Understand the underlying logic
The reasoning: issuing a fatwa or acting without a legal proof is forbidden (haram). The possible proof is either rational or transmitted. Since the matter is beyond rational reach (by assumption), transmitted proof is necessary. The Sahabi’s statement is assumed to be based on hearing (sam‘), thus it is a transmitted proof.
This logic is a rational necessity (darurah) – the absence of any other possible proof forces us to accept the Sahabi’s statement as the only available proof.
3
Trace the objections
Two objections are raised: (1) Why must a Sahabi himself follow another Sahabi’s opinion? If it were a proof, it would be binding on him too. (2) Why is a Tabi'i’s opinion not equally binding? The same need for a transmitted proof applies to him.
The objections test the consistency of the principle.
4
Follow the Hanafi response
The author answers: (1) Indeed, a Sahabi must follow another Sahabi’s opinion when the latter has a stronger basis (e.g., direct hearing). Example: Zaid ibn Arqam followed ‘Aisha’s ruling. (2) The Tabi'i did not hear directly from the Prophet and did not witness the contextual clues (qara’in) that help avoid error. Also, the justice of the Sahabah is textually established (by the Qur’an and Sunnah), whereas the justice of the Tabi’i is only conjectural. Hence the presumption of reliability is much weaker for the Tabi'i.
The key distinction: the Sahabi is a direct witness of revelation; the Tabi'i is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب التقليد وكون مذهبه في حكم المرفوع لأنه لا بد من حجة نقلية) لأن الفتوى والعمل من غير حجة شرعية حرام والصحابة بريئون عنه بعد التهم، فالحجة عقلية أو نقلية، والأول منتف بالفرض ، فتعين الثاني فله حكم الرفع فمذهبه دليل الدليل كالإجماع (ونقض بالصحابي) فإنه ينبغي أن يجب عليه التقليد أيضاً، لأن المرفوع واجب الاتباع عليه أيضاً (و) نقض بتقليد (التابعي) فيما لا يدرك بالرأي، لأنه لا بد من حجة نقلية أيضاً (أقول : التخلف) أي تخلف المدعي (ممنوع) بل يجب على الصحابي اتباع قوله : وعلينا اتباع قول التابعي فيه لا مطلقاً، بل (عند عدم الريبة باتخاذ المذهب من غير حجة، ولذا عمل زيد بن أرقم بقول أم المؤمنين (لكن للصحابة أن يرتابوا بعضهم في بعض فلا يعمل بعضهم بقول بعض (أما نحن فلا نتكلم إلا بخير ولا نرتاب فيهم بوجه لقيام الحجة على عدالتهم كالشمس على نصف النهار، فلا يجوز لنا ترك التقليد، وأما التابعي فيجوز لنا الريبة فيه أيضاً لعدم دلالة النص على عدالة التابعين، وإنما الظن باستقراء الحال فتدبر) وقد يجاب عن الثاني بأن اتخاذ الصحابي مذهباً فيما لا مجال للرأي فيه دل دلالة قاطعة أو مظنونة ظناً قوياً أنه فيه شيئاً، فهو سمع قطعي، عنده ثبوتاً، ثم هو مشاهد للقرائن فلا يخطىء في فهم المراد، فمذهب الصحابي دليل الدليل، وأما التابعي فليس هو سامعاً، فالمسموع ليس مقطوع الثبوت، وهو غير مشاهد للقرائن المفهمة، فجاز عليه الخطأ في فهم المراد، وظن ما ليس دليلاً دليلاً، العدالة غير منصوصة فاضمحل فيه ظن الدلالة على الدليل فافهم.
The obligation of taqlid and that his (the Sahabi's) madhhab is in the ruling of marfu' (raised to the Prophet). Because there must be a transmitted proof, since issuing fatwa and acting without a legal proof is forbidden, and the Companions are innocent of that after the accusation. So the proof is either rational or transmitted, and the first is negated by assumption, so the second is determined. Thus it has the ruling of raf', so his madhhab is the proof of the proof like ijma'. (And it is refuted by the Sahabi) for he should also be obligated to taqlid, because the marfu' is obligatory to follow upon him as well. (And) refuted by taqlid of the Tabi'i in matters not reached by opinion, because there must also be a transmitted proof. (I say: the failure) i.e. the failure of the claimant (is prevented). Rather, it is obligatory upon the Sahabi to follow his saying, and upon us to follow the saying of the Tabi'i in it, not absolutely, but when there is no doubt of adopting the madhhab without proof. Hence Zaid ibn Arqam acted upon the saying of the Mother of the Believers. But the Companions may doubt each other, so some do not act upon the saying of others. As for us, we speak nothing but good and do not doubt them at all due to the establishment of their justice like the sun at midday. So it is not permissible for us to abandon taqlid. As for the Tabi'i, doubt about him is possible for us as well due to the lack of textual proof on the justice of the Tabi'is, rather only conjecture from extrapolation of the situation, so reflect. And the second (objection) may be answered that the Sahabi's adoption of a madhhab in matters where opinion has no scope indicates with definitive or strong likely indication that he heard something, so it is an auditory (sam') absolute in his view in terms of thubut; moreover, he witnessed the contextual clues so he does not err in understanding the intended meaning. Thus the Sahabi's madhhab is the proof of the proof. As for the Tabi'i, he is not a hearer, so what is heard is not absolutely established in thubut, and he does not witness the contextual clues that clarify, so error in understanding the intended meaning is possible for him, and he may think what is not a proof to be a proof; his justice is not textually established, so the presumption of indication upon the proof diminishes in his case. Understand.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a proof-based argument establishing that the opinion of a Sahabi (Companion of the Prophet) in matters that cannot be arrived at by personal reasoning (such as unseen matters or rulings transmitted from the Prophet) carries the same weight as a marfu' hadith (a statement directly attributed to the Prophet). The author explains that any fatwa or action must be based on a legitimate proof, and that proof must be either rational or transmitted. Since rational proof is excluded by assumption in such matters, transmitted proof is necessary. The Sahabi's statement, being based on direct hearing from the Prophet and witnessing the context, is considered a 'proof of the proof' (dalil al-dalil), similar to ijma' (consensus). The author then addresses two objections: first, why a Sahabi himself must follow another Sahabi's opinion? He answers that even though the Sahabi could theoretically rely on his own knowledge, in practice they sometimes followed each other, as Zaid ibn Arqam followed 'Aisha's opinion. Second, why a Tabi'i (successor) is not given the same status? He explains that the Tabi'i did not hear directly from the Prophet and did not witness the revelatory context, so error in understanding is possible, and his justice is not textually established. Therefore, while we must follow the Sahabi's opinion as a binding proof, we are not obliged to follow a Tabi'i's opinion absolutely; we may doubt it unless corroborated. This passage clarifies the foundational Hanafi principle that the Sahabah's stances are authoritative in legal derivation.

Key Terms
حجة نقلية (hujjah naqliyah): A transmitted proof, i.e., a legal evidence derived from revelation (Qur'an, Sunnah) or from the consensus or statement of a Sahabi considered as transmission.
مذهب الصحابي (madhhab al-sahabi): The legal opinion or position of a Companion of the Prophet, which in this passage is argued to be a binding proof (hujjah) when the matter is not subject to personal reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the opinion of a Sahabi considered a 'proof of the proof' (dalil al-dalil)?
A) Because the Sahabi's personal ijtihad is infallible.
B) Because the Sahabi heard directly from the Prophet and witnessed the contextual clues, making his statement a strong indication of transmission.
C) Because all Sahabah are equal in knowledge, so their opinion is like ijma'.
D) Because the Sahabi's opinion is based on reason, which is a valid proof.

Key Takeaway
The opinion of a Sahabi in matters beyond personal reasoning is a binding transmitted proof (hujjah naqliyyah) because it is assumed to be based on direct hearing and contextual witnesses, making it equivalent to a marfu' hadith.

Tarjih by Importance: Prioritizing Rulings in Hanafi Usul
Listing examples of tarjih based on the importance of the ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the factors of tarjih based on the importance of rulings and apply them to resolve apparent conflicts between evidence.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, the more important ruling (e.g., prohibition over command) is given preponderance when evidence conflicts.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut outlines several rules for preferring one ruling over another based on their importance in the eyes of the Lawgiver. These rules help resolve apparent conflicts between texts. You will learn why prohibition outweighs command, why taklifi rulings dominate wadi'i, and how the principle of repelling harm (dar' al-hadd) is prioritized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of tarjih
The passage introduces multiple comparisons. For each, note which two rulings are being compared and which is deemed more important.
Importance here is determined by the Lawgiver's objectives: repelling harm (dar' al-mafasid) takes priority over bringing benefit (jalb al-manafi).
2
Understand the basis for each preference
For each pair, recognize the underlying rationale. For example, prohibition over command because repelling harm is more important than bringing benefit. Similarly, dar' al-hadd over imposition because the Prophet actively sought to avoid punishments.
These rationales are not arbitrary but rooted in Qur'anic priorities.
3
Recognize scholarly differences
Note that not all scholars agree. The passage mentions Ibn Arabi's preference for ibaha (permissibility) and another view equating haram and wajib. Understand the basis for these disagreements.
Hanafi tarjih generally prefers prohibition, but other views exist.
4
Apply the rule to a sample conflict
The Hanafi imams applied the rule that prohibition preponderates over command to resolve the apparent conflict between the hadith prohibiting prayer at disliked times and the hadith ordering missed prayers to be made up at any time. They concluded that the prohibition remains, and the command is limited.
This illustrates how tarjih rules are used in actual istinbat.

(و) الترجيح في المتن قد يكون (بالأهمية) بأن يكون الحكم المفاد بأحدهما أهم في نظر الشارع من الحكم المستفاد من الآخر، فالأهم أرجح من غيره ( كالتكليفي) من الحكم يترجح (على) الحكم الوضعي على) المذهب (الصحيح) لأن التكليفي أهم (والمقتضى للصدق على الشرعية) أي الثابت بالاقتضاء لأجل صدق الكلام يترجح على الثابت اقتضاء لأجل المشروعية عند التعارض، فإن الصدق أهم (والنهي) يترجح على الأمر، لأن دفع المفسدة المستفادة من النهي (أهم من جلب المنفعة) المستفاد من الأمر، ولذا رجح أئمتنا حديث النهي عن الصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة على قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : من نام عن صلاة أو نسيها فليصلها إذا ذكرها فإن ذلك وقتها» رواه مسلم فيما تعارضا والتحريم) يترجح على غيره من الأحكام لذلك والاحتياط، ولذا قدموا حديث النهي المذكور على حديث إباحة ذلك في الجمعة وقت الاستواء أو في مكة مطلقاً وقيل : بتقديم الإباحة وترجيحها على غيرها مطلقاً، وهو مختار الشيخ الأكبر صاحب الفتوحات قدس سره ) لأنه عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام كان يحب التخفيف على أمته والظاهر بقاء الأحكام على ما يحبه (وقيل: المحرم والموجب متساويان لا ترجيح لأحدهما على الآخر لأن ترجيح التحريم كان للاحتياط، وليس ههنا، لأن ترك الواجب وارتكاب الحرام بمنزلة واحدة هذا ومثبت درء الحد يترجح (على موجبه) لأن الدرء كان أهم ألا ترى أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم كيف كان يحتال لدرئه (وموجب الطلاق والعتاق يترجح على (نافيهما لأن موجبهما في قوة المحرم (و) يترجح (الحكم المعلل أي الحكم المذكور مع العلة على غيره) أي الحكم الذي لم يذكر لأن ذكر العلة ينادي على الأهمية وما ذكر معه السبب) يترجح على نقيضه) لأن ذكر السبب قرينة الأهمية
And preponderance in the text may be by importance: that the ruling conveyed by one is more important in the view of the Lawgiver than the ruling from the other, so the more important is preponderant. For example: the taklifi ruling (obligatory/prohibitory) is preponderant over the wadi'i (declaratory) ruling, according to the correct madhhab, because taklifi is more important. And that which is required for truthfulness (established by necessity for the truth of the statement) is preponderant over that established for the sake of legislation when they conflict, because truthfulness is more important. And prohibition is preponderant over command, because repelling harm from prohibition is more important than bringing benefit from command. Hence our imams gave preponderance to the hadith prohibiting prayer at disliked times over his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it, for that is its time.' (Muslim) when they conflict. And the forbidden (haram) is preponderant over other rulings for that reason and for precaution. Therefore they gave precedence to the mentioned prohibitory hadith over the hadith permitting it on Friday at zenith or in Mecca absolutely. And it was said: permission is preferred and given preponderance over everything, which is the choice of the great Shaykh (Ibn Arabi) because he (peace be upon him) loved ease for his community, and the apparent is that rulings remain as he loved. And it was said: the forbidden and the obligatory are equal, no preponderance of one over the other, because preponderance of prohibition was for precaution, but here it is not so since abandoning an obligatory and committing a forbidden are equivalent. And the principle affirming repelling of punishment (dar' al-hadd) is preponderant over its imposition, because repelling was more important; do you not see how the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to resort to stratagems to repel it? And what necessitates divorce and emancipation is preponderant over what negates them, because they are in the strength of prohibited. And a reasoned ruling (mentioned with its cause) is preponderant over one not mentioned with its cause, because mentioning the cause indicates importance. And what is mentioned with its reason is preponderant over its opposite, because mentioning the reason is a clue to importance.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage details several tarjih factors based on importance. Taklifi rulings (obligatory, prohibited) outweigh wadi'i (declaratory) rulings. Rulings established by necessity for truthfulness (to avoid contradiction) outweigh those for legislation. Prohibition outweighs command due to repelling harm being prioritized. The Hanafi imams used this to prefer the hadith prohibiting prayer at disliked times over the general order to make up missed prayers. Haram outweighs other categories; however, some scholars like Ibn Arabi argue for permission (ibaha) as preferred, citing the Prophet's love of ease. Another view equates haram and wajib. The passage also covers: repelling hudud penalties outweighs imposition (the Prophet used stratagems to avoid punishment), obligations of divorce or emancipation outweigh their negation (since they resemble prohibitions in strength), and a ruling mentioned with its cause or reason is preponderant over one without, as it signals importance.

Key Terms
الحكم التكليفي (al-hukm al-taklifi): A ruling that imposes a duty or prohibition on the mukallaf (e.g., wajib, haram).
الحكم الوضعي (al-hukm al-wad'i): A declaratory ruling that does not impose a duty but sets conditions (e.g., sabab, mani).
درء الحد (dar' al-hadd): Repelling, avoiding, or interpreting away the application of a prescribed punishment due to doubts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is given preponderance according to the passage?
A) Command over prohibition.
B) Declaratory over taklifi.
C) Prohibition over command.
D) Imposition of punishment over repelling it.

Key Takeaway
The importance of a ruling—determined by the Lawgiver's priorities such as repelling harm over bringing benefit—determines preponderance in tarjih, guiding how conflicting evidence is resolved.

Intrinsic vs Accidental Tarjih in Usul: The Fasting Intention Dispute
Explaining intrinsic vs accidental tarjih with fasting intention example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that intrinsic tarjih factors outweigh accidental ones to resolve competing evidences and explain the Hanafi position on the validity of a fast with intention made before noon.

Core Idea
Intrinsic qualities (most of the act) outweigh accidental ones (beginning part) in tarjih.

This passage introduces a key principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: when two tarjih factors conflict, the one intrinsic to the act itself (dhati) takes precedence over an accidental one (aradi). The author applies this to a classic dispute with the Shafi'i school over the validity of an obligatory fast when the intention is made after dawn but before noon. Understanding this principle helps you see how the Hanafi school weighs evidence holistically, not mechanically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tarjih principle
The passage states that some tarjih factors are stronger than others; specifically, intrinsic (dhati) factors are stronger than accidental (aradi) ones. Understand that 'intrinsic' refers to qualities inherent to the act’s core, while 'accidental' refers to peripheral or incidental aspects.
This is a universal usul principle, not limited to this example.
2
Analyze the conflicting factors in the fasting example
For a person who begins an obligatory fast without intention at night but intends before noon: part of the fast is intended (the majority) and part is not (the early part). The 'corrupting factor' (mufsid) is the lack of intention for the early part; the 'correcting factor' (musahhih) is the presence of intention for most of the day. These two factors conflict.
Note that the Shafi'i sees the lack of intention for any part as fatal; the Hanafi sees the presence for most as overriding.
3
Compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i tarjih choices
Al-Shafi'i gave preponderance to the corrupting factor because he holds that worship requires intention for every part. Abu Hanifa gave preponderance to the correcting factor because he holds that intention for the majority suffices, and the early part is covered retroactively. Each school applies a different view of what the act requires.
The Hanafi view is supported by the hadith of 'Ashura (where the Prophet ﷺ made intention during the day).
4
Link the tarjih choice to the intrinsic vs accidental principle
Abu Hanifa's ruling is the preponderant one because the intrinsic aspect (the majority of the fast having intention) outweighs the accidental (the early part lacking intention). The majority (akthar) is considered the core of the act, making it dhati; the minority is 'aradi.
This shows how the principle is applied: the 'most' is given weight over the 'less'.
5
Extract the broader usul lesson
The passage teaches that in usul al-fiqh, when balancing conflicting indications (ta'arud al-adilla), the intrinsic quality (being part of the essence) is a stronger tarjih factor than an accidental one. This is a tool for muftis to prefer one evidence over another.
This principle is used across many fiqh chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يكون بعضها أي بعض التراجيح أولى من بعض آخر فيرجح عند التعارض (كالذاتي من العرضي أي كالترجيح الواقع من الذاتي، فإنه أقوى من الترجيح الواقع من العرضي (مثل صوم معين كصوم شهر رمضان أو النذر المعين نوى قبل النصف من اليوم فلم يبيت من الليل (فبعضه منوي) وهو الإمساك الواقع بعد النية وبعضه (لا) وهو الإمساك الواقع قبلها من أول اليوم (ولا) تجزىء) في الصوم الواجب بالاتفاق فتعارض مفسد الكل) وهو فساد بعض الأجزاء بفقدان النية ( ومصححه) وهو الجزء المنوي فرجح الشافعي الأول، لأن العبادة تقتضي النية في الكل) وقد انعدمت ونحن لا نساعد عليه فاقتضاء العبادة النية في الجملة مسلم واقتضاؤها قبل كل جزء ممنوع بل العبادات متنوعة منها ما يجب فيه لصوقها أول الأجزاء كالصلاة، ومنها ما يكفي فيه لصوقها بأكثر الأجزاء ويبقى الأقل موقوفاً على ما بعدها، نعم : لا من حجة، وقد تقدم في حديث صوم عاشوراء (وأبو حنيفة) رجح (الثاني: لأن للأكثر بد لذلك
And sometimes some tarjih factors are preferable over others, so they are given preponderance when conflicting (like the intrinsic over the accidental): the preponderance due to an intrinsic quality is stronger than that due to an accidental one. (Example): a specific obligatory fast like that of Ramadan or a specific vow: he intends before half of the day but did not make the intention at night. Then part of it (the fast) is intended (i.e., the abstention after the intention) and part of it is not (i.e., the abstention from the beginning of the day until the intention). And it (the fast) is not valid in obligatory fasting by consensus (if no intention before dawn). So there is a conflict between a corrupting factor (i.e., the corruption of some parts due to lack of intention) and a correcting factor (i.e., the intended part). Al-Shafi'i preferred the first (i.e., the fasting is invalid) because the act of worship requires intention in all of it, and it is lacking. But we (Hanafis) do not agree with this: the requirement of intention for worship in general is accepted, but its requirement for each part is denied. Rather, acts of worship differ: some require intention attached to the first parts, like prayer; others suffice with intention attached to most parts, and the minor part remains dependent on the later part. Yes, there is no proof for that, and it has preceded in the hadith of fasting 'Ashura. And Abu Hanifah gave preponderance to the second (i.e., the fast is valid) because the majority (of the fast) has a substitute for that.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage discusses a principle of tarjih: intrinsic (dhati) factors are stronger than accidental (aradi) ones. It applies this to a fiqh problem: a person begins an obligatory fast (Ramadan or a specific vow) without intending at night, but forms the intention before noon (half of the day). The Shafi'i ruling is that the fast is invalid because intention is required for the entire fast. The Hanafi ruling (Abu Hanifa) is that the fast is valid because intention is required for the majority of the day, and the first part is covered by the later intention. Abu Hanifa's preponderance is based on the principle that the intrinsic (the intention for most of the day) outweighs the accidental (the lack of intention in the early part). The passage mentions that acts of worship vary: some require intention at the very start (like prayer), while for fasting, intention during the day suffices (as evidenced by the hadith of 'Ashura). The 'second' (Abu Hanifa's view) is preferred because the 'most' (most of the fast) has a substitute for the early part (i.e., the later intention covers it).

Key Terms
الذاتي والعرضي (al-dhati wa al-'aradi): Intrinsic (inherent to the thing itself) vs accidental (external) qualities; intrinsic is stronger in tarjih

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Hanifa rule that an obligatory fast with intention made before noon is valid, while al-Shafi'i ruled it invalid?
A) Abu Hanifa believed intention is not required at all
B) Abu Hanifa considered the intrinsic quality of having intention for most of the day to outweigh the accidental lack at the start
C) Al-Shafi'i relied on a hadith that Abu Hanifa rejected
D) Abu Hanifa considered the fast as a single continuous act

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when tarjih factors conflict, the intrinsic quality (dhati) of an act takes precedence over accidental (aradi) aspects, as demonstrated by the validity of a fast with intention made before noon.
