Essential vs Accidental Tarjih: The Fasting Intention Example
Explains the superiority of essential tarjih using fasting intention dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dhati and aradi tarjih and apply the rule that dhati tarjih prevails in case of conflict.

Core Idea
Essential (dhati) tarjih overrides accidental (aradi) because it stems from the act's own parts.

In Hanafi usul, when two evidences conflict, we give preference (tarjih) to the stronger one. But not all tarjih aspects are equal: some are essential (dhati) to the act itself, while others are accidental (aradi). This passage uses the classic dispute over intention in fasting to illustrate that dhati tarjih is inherently stronger. You will learn to identify these qualities and see why the Hanafi position is preferred.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify that the passage establishes: essential (dhati) tarjih is stronger than accidental (aradi) tarjih when they conflict.
This is the core usul rule being taught.
2
Recognize the example
The example is a person who begins an obligatory fast (Ramadan or a specific vow) without intention at night but intends before midday. The fast is partially intended (after intention) and partially not (before intention).
3
Isolate the two conflicting arguments
Al-Shafi'i argues the fast is invalid because worship requires intention for the whole act. Abu Hanifa argues the fast is valid because the majority of the day is intended, citing the Ashura precedent.
These two arguments represent the tarjih conflict.
4
Classify each argument as dhati or aradi
The Shafi'i argument is based on the general attribute of 'ibadah (worship) requiring intention — this is accidental (aradi). The Hanafi argument focuses on the parts of the fast itself — this is essential (dhati).
The author notes that according to Hanafi usul, even the attribute of 'ibadah is not truly accidental, but the analysis of the parts is clearly dhati.
5
Apply the rule
Since dhati tarjih is stronger, the Hanafi position prevails: the fast is valid with intention before midday.
This demonstrates how the rule determines the outcome in a real ikhtilaf.

وقد يكون بعضها أي بعض التراجيح أولى من بعض آخر فيرجح عند التعارض (كالذاتي من العرضي أي كالترجيح الواقع من الذاتي، فإنه أقوى من الترجيح الواقع من العرضي (مثل صوم معين كصوم شهر رمضان أو النذر المعين نوى قبل النصف من اليوم فلم يبيت من الليل (فبعضه منوي) وهو الإمساك الواقع بعد النية وبعضه (لا) وهو الإمساك الواقع قبلها من أول اليوم (ولا) تجزىء) في الصوم الواجب بالاتفاق فتعارض مفسد الكل) وهو فساد بعض الأجزاء بفقدان النية ( ومصححه) وهو الجزء المنوي فرجح الشافعي الأول، لأن العبادة تقتضي النية في الكل) وقد انعدمت ونحن لا نساعد عليه فاقتضاء العبادة النية في الجملة مسلم واقتضائها قبل كل جزء ممنوع بل العبادات متنوعة منها ما يجب فيه لصوقها أول الأجزاء كالصلاة، ومنها ما يكفي فيه لصوقها بأكثر الأجزاء ويبقى الأقل موقوفاً على ما بعدها، نعم : لا من حجة، وقد تقدم في حديث صوم عاشوراء (وأبو حنيفة) رجح (الثاني: لأن للأكثر كان حكم الكل في مواضع منها هذا بدليل تصحيح صوم عاشوراء المنوي في اليوم حين فرضاً وهذا ذاتي) لأنه بالأجزاء، بخلاف الترجيح بالعبادة لكونه بالعرضي (أقول في كون العبادة وصفاً عرضياً للحقيقة الشرعية للصوم نظر فتدبر ) بل لا يصح على رأينا، لأن الصوم عبادة عندنا عما اعتبره الشارع ،عبادة كما مر في مسائل النهي بل الوجه أنه لم يوجد ههنا مفسد الكل أصلاً، فإن القدر المشروط من النية قد وجد
And some of them, i.e., some of the tarjih aspects, are more deserving than others, so preference is given in case of conflict (like the essential over the accidental). For example, a specific fast like the fast of Ramadan or a specific vow: he intended before the middle of the day, but did not form the intention at night. So part of it (the fast) is intended (the abstention after the intention) and part is not (the abstention from the beginning of the day). And it is not valid in obligatory fasting by agreement. So the corruptor of the whole (the corruption of some parts due to lack of intention) and the rectifier (the intended part) conflict. Al-Shafi'i gave preference to the former because worship requires intention in the whole, and it is missing. But we do not agree with this; the requirement of intention in worship in general is conceded, but the requirement before each part is denied. Rather, acts of worship are diverse: some require intention attached to the first parts, like prayer; some suffice with intention attached to most parts, and the remaining part is dependent on what follows. Yes, there is no proof from the hadith of fasting Ashura. Abu Hanifa gave preference to the second, because the majority have the ruling of the whole in some cases, among them this one, evidenced by the validation of fasting Ashura intended during the day when it was obligatory. And this is an essential (dhati) tarjih because it is based on parts, unlike the tarjih by worship (ibadah) which is accidental. I say: There is consideration in considering worship an accidental attribute of the legal reality of fasting, so reflect. Rather, it is not correct according to our view, because fasting is worship in our view due to what the Lawgiver considered as worship, as mentioned in the chapters on prohibitions. Rather, the correct view is that there was no corruptor of the whole at all here, because the required amount of intention is present.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage explains the rule that essential (dhati) tarjih is stronger than accidental (aradi) tarjih. The example is a person who begins an obligatory fast (like Ramadan or a specific vow) without an intention at night but forms the intention before midday. The fast is partially valid (from the time of intention) and partially invalid (from dawn to intention). There is a conflict: the invalid part corrupts the whole? The Shafi'i view says the fast is invalid because intention is required for the entire fast. The Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa) says the intention suffices for the majority of the day, and the fast is valid, based on the precedent of Ashura where the Prophet commanded fasting during the day. The author classifies this as a dhati tarjih because it relates to the parts of the act itself, whereas the Shafi'i tarjih is based on the attribute of 'ibadah, which is accidental. The author then critiques the view that ibadah is accidental, asserting that according to Hanafi usul, the requirement of intention is already fulfilled.

Key Terms
ذاتي (dhati): Essential or intrinsic quality; a point of tarjih that stems from the nature of the act itself.
عرضي (aradi): Accidental or extrinsic quality; a point of tarjih that is not inherent to the act.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preference or weighting given to one evidence or argument over another when they conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of fasting, which type of tarjih did Abu Hanifa use to validate the fast?
A) Aradi tarjih
B) Dhati tarjih
C) Tarjih by majority
D) Tarjih by direct involvement

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, essential (dhati) tarjih inherently prevails over accidental (aradi) tarjih, as demonstrated by the intention in fasting dispute.

Definition and Conceivability of Ijma
Defining ijma and defending its conceivability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi technical definition of ijma and explain how the Hanafi school defends its conceivability against the classical objections.

Core Idea
Ijma is the agreement of mujtahids of the ummah in one era on a religious matter; its conceivability is defended by focusing on existing mujtahids.

This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut introduces the third foundational principle of usul al-fiqh: ijma (consensus). You will learn both the linguistic roots and the precise technical definition used by the Hanafi school. More importantly, you will see how the Hanafis respond to classical objections that ijma is inconceivable or non-universal. By the end, you will understand why the definition is carefully circumscribed to mujtahids in an era.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic and technical definitions
First, note the two linguistic meanings (determination and agreement) and see how they both derive from 'gathering'. Then extract the technical definition from the text: 'agreement of the mujtahids of this ummah in an era upon a religious matter'.
The hadith and Quranic verse support the linguistic meaning.
2
Identify the two points of disagreement about conditions
The passage mentions two stipulations: (1) whether agreement must continue until the end of the era, and (2) how to treat a predecessor's differing view. The commentator clarifies these relate to ijma's authority (hujjiyyah), not its definition.
The definition itself is agreed upon; the conditions are about when it becomes binding.
3
Examine the objections to Imam al-Ghazali's definition
Imam al-Ghazali defined ijma as 'agreement of the ummah of Muhammad on a religious matter'. Two objections were raised: first, it is inconceivable because we cannot know future agreement; second, it is not universal if no mujtahid exists among them. The passage shows that only the second objection applies.
The first objection fails because the immediate understanding of 'ummah' is the existing ones at the time.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the valid objection
The second objection claims that if there are no mujtahids, then agreement of non-mujtahids would be called ijma. The response: the basis of the counterexample (empty era of mujtahids) is contrary to reality. Also, the definition can be refined to 'mujtahids' as in the hadith 'My ummah will not agree upon error'.
The hadith supports defining ijma as agreement of mujtahids, since the word 'ummah' in context implies the scholars.
5
Confirm the definition's validity
By focusing on existing mujtahids in any era, the definition becomes conceivable and universal. The passage concludes that the correct technical definition is that given at the start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثالث: الإجماع (وهو لغة : العزم كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاجْمِعُوا أَمْرَكُمْ ﴾ [يونس: ٧١] وفي قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : لا صيام لمن لم يجمع الصيام من الليل» (والاتفاق وكلاهما) أي الذي بمعنى العزم والذي بمعنى الاتفاق مأخوذان من الجمع) فإن العزم فيه جمع الخواطر، الآراء واصطلاحاً : اتفاق المجتهدين من هذه الأمة المرحومة المكرمة (في عصر على أمر شرعي ومن يشترط انقراض العصر يزيد عليه اتفاقاً مستمراً إلى الانقراض ومن خلاف السابق يزيد بحسبه والحق أن الحد هذا والشارط لأحد الأمرين إنما عدم يشترط، سيأتي، یرد يشترط للحجية فافهم. قال الإمام (حجة الإسلام قدس سره الإجماع (اتفاق أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم على أمر ديني وأورد كما في «المختصر» أنه لا يتصور) لأن اتفاق من سيجيء لم يعلم بعد (وأنه لا يطرد إن لم يكن فيهم مجتهد) ووقع اتفاق عدا من المجتهد، فإنه يصدق عليه اتفاق الأمة وليس إجماعاً أقول) الموجود من الأمة أمة أم لا وعلى الأول فاتفاق الأمة وهم الموجودون متصوّر قطعاً، ولا عبرة لمن فلا الأول، وعلى الثاني : فلا مجال لأن يتوهم إن لم يمض مجتهد ولا يجيء في المستقبل، فلا يرد الثاني (فالوارد أحد الإيرادين لا كلاهما والحق) ورود (الثاني لأن المتبادر من الأمة الموجود منهم (والجواب عنه أن مادة النقض يجب تحققها، وهو ههنا ممنوع فإن خلو كل عصر عن المجتهد خلاف الواقع (وقد يدفعان بإرادة اتفاق المجتهدين في عصر، لأنه المتبادر إلى الفهم في هذا المقام (كما في قوله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام: «لا تجتمع أمني على الضلالة وإنما اختار هذا التجوز إحرازاً لحسن الاقتباس فافهم.
The third foundational principle: Ijma (consensus). (It is linguistically: determination as in His statement: 'So determine your plan' [Yunus:71] and in his statement, peace be upon him and his family and companions: 'No fasting for the one who does not determine the fast from the night') (and agreement; both meanings) i.e., that which means determination and that which means agreement, both derived from 'gathering' for determination involves gathering of thoughts, opinions. And technically: agreement of the mujtahids of this blessed and honored ummah in an era upon a religious matter. And whoever stipulates the passing of the era adds thereto 'continuous agreement until the [era's] passing.' And whoever differs from the predecessor adds accordingly. The correct [definition] is this, and the one stipulating one of the two conditions only stipulates for authoritative force, so understand. Imam (Hujjat al-Islam, may Allah sanctify his soul) said: Ijma is the agreement of the ummah of Muhammad, peace be upon him and his family and companions, upon a religious matter. And it was objected, as in al-Mukhtasar, that it is inconceivable because the agreement of those who will come is not yet known, and that it is not universal if there is no mujtahid among them, and that if agreement occurs among non-mujtahids, it would apply to the agreement of the ummah yet it is not ijma. I say: The existing ones from the ummah are [all] the ummah or not? On the first, the agreement of the ummah, i.e., the existing ones, is certainly conceivable, and no attention is paid to [the rest] so the first [objection] falls; on the second, there is no room for the illusion that no mujtahid has passed or will come, so the second [objection] does not apply. So only one of the two objections applies, not both, and the correct [view] is that the second applies because the immediate understanding from 'the ummah' is the existing ones. The answer to it is that the basis of the counterexample must be realized, and here it is denied, for the emptiness of every era from a mujtahid is contrary to reality. And both can be repelled by intending the agreement of the mujtahids in any era, because it is the immediate understanding in this context, as in his statement, peace be upon him and his family and companions: 'My ummah will not agree upon error,' and he chose this metaphorical expression to secure good quotation, so understand.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut, explaining the third principle of usul: Ijma. It opens with the linguistic definition of ijma (determination and agreement), citing Quran and hadith. Then it gives the technical definition: the agreement of mujtahids of the ummah in an era on a religious ruling. It notes two points of disagreement: whether the agreement must continue until the end of the era, and how to treat a predecessor's differing view. The commentator clarifies that the stipulation of these conditions relates to ijma's authority, not its definition. Then it presents and responds to objections against Imam al-Ghazali's definition (agreement of the ummah). The objections are that it is inconceivable (because future generations are unknown) and that it is not universal (if no mujtahid exists). The response shows that only one objection applies, and it is answered by noting that the immediate understanding of 'ummah' refers to existing mujtahids, and that the hadith 'My ummah will not agree upon error' supports this metaphorical usage.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijmā‘): اتفاق المجتهدين من الأمة في عصر على أمر شرعي

D) Agreement of the scholars of a single city

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi technical definition of ijma is precise: agreement of mujtahids of the ummah in one era on a religious matter, and its conceivability is defended by focusing on existing mujtahids.

Definition of 'Illa and Its Rationale: Hanafi Response to the Mutakallimun
Introduces 'illa definition and defends teleological rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'illa, explain why rulings are rationalized by masalih, and evaluate the mutakallimun objection of istikmal.

Core Idea
The 'illa is a branch of Allah's perfection, not a completion; rationalizing rulings by benefit affirms His wisdom and mercy.

This passage defines the 'illa (effective cause) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh and engages a critical theological dispute: are legal rulings truly rationalized by the interests of the servants? The author presents the Hanafi affirmation and refutes the objection that it would imply Allah's completion (istikmal). Understanding this foundation is essential for appreciating how Hanafis derive rulings via qiyas using suitable attributes (al-munasib).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'illa
Locate the phrase 'ما شرع الحكم عنده تحصيلاً للمصلحة' – 'That for which the ruling is legislated to achieve benefit.' Note that 'illa is the attribute that motivates the ruling for bringing benefit or repelling harm.
This definition ties 'illa to maslahah, a teleological component.
2
Understand the foundational principle: rulings are based on masalih
The text asserts that shar'i rulings are 'معللة بمصالح العباد' (rationalized by the interests of the servants). This is a theological premise: Allah legislates to benefit His creation out of grace.
Contrast with Ash'ari view that rulings need not be for any purpose.
3
Recognize the mutakallimun objection: istikmal
The opponent argues that if rulings depend on interests, then Allah would be completed by those interests (استكمال). This leads some to deny qiyas entirely or treat 'illa as a mere sign (al-tard).
The objection is about attributing need or dependency to Allah.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response: 'illa as branch of perfection
The author responds: 'بل فرع الكمال' – observing interests is a branch of Allah's perfect attributes (wisdom, generosity, mercy). It does not complete Him but flows from His perfection.
Hanafis distinguish between completion (istikmal) and manifestation (far'iyah).
5
Connect to practical effect
Because 'illa is rooted in divine attributes, Hanafis can identify suitable attributes (munasib) for qiyas. This allows derivation of rulings for new cases.
Direct consequence: Hanafi qiyas is broader than strict textual analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في العلة (وهي ههنا إنما قيد به إشارة إلى أنها تطلق في غير هذا الفن على معنى آخر (ما شرع الحكم عنده تحصيلاً للمصلحة) من جلب نفع أو دفع مفسدة (وذلك مبني على أن الأحكام) الشرعية أي تعلقاتها (معللة بمصالح العباد والشارع إنما حكم بها على ما اقتضته مصالح العباد (تفضلاً منه تعالى على عباده كالآية المخلوقة لهم أي لانتفاعهم على الوحدانية والرسالة، ليستدلوا بها عليها فيصدقوا بها وينالوا السعادة القصوى. وإذا كان التعليل بالمصالح التي تعود إلى العباد لينالوا بها كما لا تهم ويهتدوا بها إلى مصالحهم الأخروية والدنيوية (فلزوم الاستكمال أي استكماله تعالى بتلك المصالح كما زعم أكثر المتكلمين) حتى منعوا التعليل في العلل المؤثرة وقالوا ليست الأحكام معللة بالمصالح أصلاً منهم من ضل ونفى ثبوت الحكم بالقياس مطلقاً، ومنهم من اكتفى بالطرد وقال : ليست العلل إلا أمارات على الأحكام، وليست داعية إليها (ممنوع) فإن منفعة التعليل بالمصالح ترجع إليهم، ثم لما كان للسائل أن يعود ويقول : إن رعاية مصالح العباد نسبتها إلى أحكامه تعالى وعدمها سواء فليست داعية إلى الأحكام. وإلا توقف كونه تعالى حاكماً عليها فقد استكمل بها، فعاد المصنف وزاد لدفعه قوله : (بل فرع الكمال يعني رعاية المنافع وحكمه تعالى على حسبها فرع كماله تعالى وتحقيقه أنه سبحانه لما كان حكيماً لا بد لأفعاله وأحكامه غايات تترتب عليها ولما كان جواداً محضاً رحماناً رحيماً اقتضى جوده ورحمته أن يراعي مصالح مخلوقاته فلا جرم حكم على ما هو مقتضى المصالح، فالأحكام المتعلقة باقتضاء المصالح فرع حكمته وجوده ورحمته ومن لوازمه، فرعاية المصالح فرح لكماله فإن قلت لا بد من اختيار أحد شقي الترديد، قلنا: نختار الثاني ونقول : إن رعاية المصالح من اللوازم فليس نسبتها إليه كنسبة عدمها، ولا يلزم الاستكمال بها، بل هم من فروع الصفات الكمالية من الرحمة والجود والحكمة ومن لوازمها، فافهم وتثبت (وفقه المقام أنه لما اقتضى هو سبحانه من عنايته التي اقتضتها الرحمة والحكمة السعادة الأبدية للناس) في الدارين (ناطها بأحكام معقولة التناسب على ما اقتضته حكمته (وذلك) أي النوط بها أنه لما أوجدهم أجساماً عقلاء أوجب عليهم المعرفة بذاته وصفاته وسائر
Section: On the Effective Cause ('Illa). (And it is here qualified to indicate that it is used in other disciplines with another meaning:) That for which the ruling is legislated to achieve benefit (maslahah) – either bringing benefit or repelling harm. That is based on the fact that the rulings (shar'i) – i.e., their attachments – are rationalized by the interests of the servants, and the Lawgiver only ruled according to what the interests of the servants required, as a favor from Him to His servants, like the signs created for them for their benefit: to indicate oneness and prophethood, so that they may infer and believe and attain ultimate happiness. And if rationalization by interests that revert to the servants is for them to attain benefits and be guided to their worldly and otherworldly interests, then the necessary consequence of completion (istikmal) – i.e., His completion by those benefits as claimed by most theologians – so much so that they denied rationalization in effective causes and said that rulings are not rationalized by interests at all. Among them are those who went astray and denied the validity of analogy absolutely; among them are those who relied on mere coincidence (tard) and said causes are only indicators of rulings, not motivators. (This is) denied, because the benefit of rationalization by interests reverts to them. Then, since the questioner may return and say: The observance of the interests of servants – its relation to His rulings and its absence are equal, so they are not motivators for the rulings; otherwise, His being a ruler over them would depend on them, so He would be completed by them. So the author returns and adds to refute it by saying: (Rather, it is a branch of perfection.) That is, the observance of benefits and His ruling in accordance with them is a branch of His perfection. Its verification: Since He is Wise, His actions and rulings must have goals that result from them, and since He is pure Generosity, Merciful, and Compassionate, His generosity and mercy required that He observes the interests of His creatures. So He inevitably ruled according to what interests require. Thus, the rulings related to the requirement of interests are branches of His wisdom, generosity, and mercy, and from their necessities. So the observance of interests is a consequence of His perfection. If you say: One of the two sides of the disjunction must be chosen. We say: We choose the second and say that the observance of interests is among the necessities, so its relation to Him is not like its absence, nor does completion by them follow; rather, they are branches of the perfect attributes (mercy, generosity, wisdom) and their necessities. So understand and be firm. And the fiqh of the situation is that when He – exalted – due to His care that mercy and wisdom necessitated, willed eternal happiness for people in both abodes, He attached it to rulings that are rationally proportionate according to His wisdom. That is, the attachment is that when He created them as rational bodies, He obligated upon them knowledge of His essence, attributes, and all
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage begins the section on the effective cause ('illa) in usul al-fiqh. The 'illa is defined as the attribute for which a ruling is legislated to achieve a benefit (maslahah), either bringing benefit or repelling harm. The discussion then addresses the theological foundation: rulings are rationalized by the interests of people. This is based on the principle that the Lawgiver (Allah) acts to benefit His servants out of grace. The author then refutes the view of the theologians (mutakallimun) who deny that rulings are rationalized by interests, fearing it would imply that Allah is completed by those interests. The author argues that observing benefits is not for Allah's completion but is rather a branch of His perfect attributes of wisdom, generosity, and mercy. Thus, the 'illa is not an independent motivator but a manifestation of divine attributes. The section ends by linking this to the broader purpose: the rulings are designed to guide people to eternal happiness.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause in qiyas; the attribute for which a ruling is legislated to achieve benefit.
المصلحة (al-maslahah): Benefit; the interest of the servant that the ruling aims to achieve.
الاستكمال (al-istikmal): Completion; the mistaken notion that Allah would be completed by the interests of His servants.
الطرد (al-tard): Mere coincidence; a sign that coincides with the ruling without being its cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, why does the observation of interests (masalih) not imply completion (istikmal) of Allah?
A) Because interests are irrelevant to His rulings.
B) Because observing interests is a branch of His perfect attributes.
C) Because the 'illa is purely conventional.
D) Because mutakallimun agree it is permissible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi 'illa is a teleological attribute that manifests divine wisdom and mercy, enabling rational derivation of rulings through qiyas.

The Hanafi Principle: Purposes Considered for the Type, Not Individuals
Classifying purposes and defending the type-based approach

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that rulings are legislated for the general type (kull/naw') to resolve apparent contradictions in cases like istibra' and travel licenses.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, purposes of rulings are attached to the type, not to each specific instance.

This passage introduces a crucial Hanafi usul principle: when a ruling is legislated to achieve a purpose, that purpose is considered for the general category (naw') of cases, not for every individual occurrence. This explains why a sale is valid even when no need exists, or why a luxury king breaks his fast. We will explore the classification of purposes and how this principle resolves disputes like istibra'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories of purpose achievement
The passage classifies rulings based on whether the purpose is achieved with certainty (yaqin), probability (zann), doubt (shakk), or illusion (wahm). Identify each with its example.
Sale = certainty; qisas = probability; hadd wine = doubt (contested); marriage of post-menopausal = illusion.
2
Understand the objection of the 'third and fourth' classes
Some scholars argue that a ruling should not be legislated if its purpose is absent in a given case. Locate this objection in the passage.
They use the example of sale when no need is apparent.
3
Analyze the Hanafi refutation based on type (kulliyyat)
The author counters that rulings are attached to the type (naw') of cases, not individuals. Confirm this with the examples of sale and the king's travel.
Purpose is considered for the type; individual exceptions do not nullify the ruling.
4
Examine the istibra' dispute as a concrete application
Abu Hanifa requires istibra' even when certainty that the womb is empty, because ownership is a mazanna (probable indication) for the type. The majority disagrees. Trace the argument in the passage.
The Hanafi position is defended using the type principle.
5
Conclude that objections based on individual exceptions are invalid
The text ends by stating that arguing against the principle with particular examples (like a sale without need) is useless because purposes derive from the type.
This solidifies the Hanafi methodology.

The second division: The purpose intended by legislating a ruling: either it is achieved with certainty upon its occurrence (like sale) was legislated (for ownership) and it is achieved immediately with certainty, (or) it is achieved with probability after it, like qisas (retaliation) was legislated (for deterrence) from committing murder, and it is achieved in most cases, for those who refrain from murder (are more) than those who commit it, (or) it is achieved (with doubt) and no example is known in the shari'a (and it is exemplified by the hadd of wine) legislated for deterrence, even though the wine drinkers are like those who refrain (But there is something in it: the equality between drinkers and refrainers is disputed. Thus in 'al-Hashiya': also, the lack of deterrence and indulgence in drinking is perhaps due to laxity in executing its hadd; if it were executed, most would refrain, so understand). (Or) it is achieved (with illusion, like the marriage of a post-menopausal woman, for the lack of offspring) from her (is more likely) and marriage was legislated for offspring. The third and fourth (class of scholars) objected, since there is no benefit in legislating a ruling that does not lead to its intended purpose; indeed, its legislation is far from the Wise. (It was refuted) by saying that a sale when non-need (appears) likely (is not invalidated by consensus) even though sale was legislated for need, because it is of the haji type. (And the travel of a luxury king is granted a license) to break the fast (definitely) even though apparently there is no hardship, and the license was granted for hardship. (As for when the purpose is definitively absent, as in the case of attributing a child to a man whose wife was in the West while he was in the East, as is the view of Abu Hanifa, due to the existence of the cause (firash), even though physical meeting is impossible and the possibility of a miracle is too remote to be considered, for the discussion is about what appears to be absent. (And regarding the obligation of istibra' (waiting one menstrual cycle) on the seller) of a slave girl, (and on the buyer) of her (in the session) even though it is certain that her womb is not occupied with the buyer's fluid, and istibra' was only for the possibility of occupation, (so it is not considered by the majority, contrary to Abu Hanifa, as the Shafi'is derived from these two cases. (Because) he considered the firash as a cause for lineage and the occurrence of ownership as a cause for istibra', because they are probable indications (mazanna) of the child being from him and of the womb being occupied with his water. But there is no consideration given to a probable indication when its actualization (mi'anna) is definitely absent. (I say) this is refuted by the case of the luxury king's travel (when hardship is definitely absent, he is still granted license required definitely, so the probable indication was considered even when its actualization is definitely absent). Likewise, it is refuted by the case of a divorced woman who has not been touched, after giving birth to a child six months later, for it is necessary to be certain of no occupation, but divorce only required the 'idda (waiting period) because it is a probable indication of occupation. The correct solution is that purposes are only considered in the legislation (of rulings) (as a whole (kulliyan), so they must be attached to the type (naw'). So if the type is such that purposes attach to it, it becomes a valid probable indication, even if it does not attach to some individuals. So we do not accept that a probable indication is disregarded due to the definite absence of its actualization with respect to the essence? Rather, a probable indication is not disregarded due to the absence of actualization with respect to the 'whatness' (mahiyya) along with the type. And this is not necessary, for lineage attaches to firash and occurrence of ownership attaches to the possibility of occupation, even if they are missing in some cases. From this, it becomes clear to you that the derivation of the occurrence of legislating a ruling whose purpose does not attach to its type from these two cases, and its attribution to this great Imam (Abu Hanifa), is not correct. (From this, i.e., from what we have stated that consideration of purposes is only in the legislation of general rulings, it becomes clear that arguing against the denier of the third and fourth (class) with a particular example like a sale without need or the king's travel (is useless), for purposes are derived from the type definitely or commonly, so understand).
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents the second classification of the purposes of rulings: they are either achieved with certainty (yaqin), overwhelming probability (zann), doubt (shakk), or illusion (wahm). Examples: sale for certainty, qisas for probability, hadd of wine for doubt (though the text notes criticism of this example), and marriage of a post-menopausal woman for illusion. The text then addresses an objection from scholars who deny the validity of ruling where the purpose is absent (third and fourth classes). It refutes them by arguing that purposes are considered for the type (kull/naw') of cases, not each individual. Even if a specific sale is not needed, the ruling stands because generally sales serve a need. Similarly, a luxury king is allowed to break fast even if no hardship, because the type (travel) usually involves hardship. The text defends Abu Hanifa's view on istibra' for a buyer: even when certain that the womb is empty, istibra' is required because ownership is a probable indication. The majority opposed this, but the author argues that considering the type justifies the rule. The passage ends by asserting that objections based on individual exceptions do not invalidate the general principle.

Key Terms
مظنة (mazanna): A probable indication or context that typically leads to the existence of a legal cause, even if not certain.
مئنة (mi'anna): The actual realization or occurrence of the cause; the definite existence of the purpose.
كليات (kulliyyat): The general type (naw') of cases, as opposed to individual instances (juz'iyyat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the license to break the fast granted to a luxury king who experiences no hardship?
A) Because the purpose of the license is certainty of hardship.
B) Because the license is granted for the type of travel, not the individual's experience.
C) Because the king can choose to break the fast regardless.
D) Because the purpose is achieved with doubt in his case.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, rulings are legislated for the general type (naw'), not for each individual instance; therefore, a ruling remains valid even if the purpose is absent in a specific case.

Hanafi Taxonomy of Legal Descriptors
Introducing the Hanafi classification of legal descriptors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four Hanafi legal descriptors (`illah, sabab, shart, `alamah) and classify a given description into one of these categories.

Core Idea
A legal descriptor either causes, leads, conditions, or merely indicates the ruling.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding how a descriptor relates to the ruling is crucial for correct legal reasoning. This passage introduces the four categories of legal descriptors: the cause (`illah), reason (sabab), condition (shart), and sign (`alamah). Mastering this taxonomy will enable you to identify the precise role a description plays in the derivation of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall framework
The Hanafis classify any description external to the thing (avoiding the pillars) that relates to a ruling into four types.
The description must be external to the thing, meaning it is not a constituent part (rukn).
2
Define the cause (`illah)
A cause is effective in the ruling and impelling towards it. It directly influences the existence of the ruling.
The `illah is the basis for qiyas; it must have sufficiency and suitability.
3
Define the reason (sabab)
A reason leads to the ruling without direct effect; an intervening `illah sits between the sabab and the ruling.
The sabab is a path to the ruling, but the ruling is not directly caused by it.
4
Distinguish condition (shart) and sign (`alamah)
A condition is something upon which the ruling's existence depends, but it does not cause or lead. A sign is a pure indicator with no causal connection.
The sign is merely a marker; e.g., the shadow indicates the time for prayer but does not cause its obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكملة : الحنفية (قالوا الوصف (الخارج عن الشيء احترازاً عن الأركان المتعلق بالحكم إما مؤثر فيه وباعث عليه (وهو العلة وتقدم بأقسامها أو مفض إليه بلا تأثير) فيه (وهو السبب وقد يطلق مجازاً على (العلة أيضاً (أولاً) يكون مؤثراً ولا مفضياً فإن توقف عليه وجوده فالشرط) وإن لم يتوقف فلا بد أن يكون دالاً محضاً، فهو المسمى بالعلامة كما قال : (وإن دل فالعلامة، ثم كل سبب طريق للحكم ومفض إليه ويتخلل العلة بينه وبينه) أي بين الحكم وبين السبب. فإنه لو لم تتخلل العلة ولم توجد لم يوجد الحكم قطعاً فلا إفضاء أصلاً (فإن أضيف إليه العلة بأن يكون موجباً للعلة المؤثرة في الشيء (فهو) السبب (في معنى العلة،
Supplementary: The Hanafis said: The description external to the thing (to avoid the pillars) related to the ruling is either effective in it and impelling towards it—that is the cause, and its divisions have preceded—or leads to it without effectiveness—that is the reason, and it may be used metaphorically for the cause as well. Or it is neither effective nor leading; if the ruling's existence depends on it, it is the condition. If it does not depend, it must be a pure indicator, and it is called the sign, as he said: 'If it indicates, it is the sign.' Then every reason is a path to the ruling and leads to it, with the cause intervening between it and the ruling. For if the cause does not intervene and does not exist, the ruling does not exist at all, so there is no leading. If the cause is added to it such that it necessitates the effective cause of the thing, then the reason is in the meaning of the cause.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi classification of the types of legal descriptors (wasf) that relate to a ruling. The Hanafis divide descriptors into four categories: (1) The cause (`illah) which directly effects and impels the ruling. (2) The reason (sabab) which leads to the ruling but without direct effect; the `illah intervenes between the sabab and the ruling. For example, entering into a contract (sabab) leads to ownership (`illah) which then effects the ruling. (3) The condition (shart) upon which the existence of the ruling depends, but it is not effective. (4) The sign (`alamah) which merely indicates the ruling without any causal link. Importantly, the sabab is distinct from the `illah; the ruling is not directly caused by the sabab but through an intervening `illah. If the sabab itself necessitates the `illah, it is considered in the meaning of a cause. This classification is foundational in Hanafi usul al-fiqh for understanding how legal rulings are derived.

Key Terms
العلة (al-illah): The effective cause: a description that directly influences and impels the ruling.
السبب (al-sabab): A reason that leads to the ruling through an intervening cause, without direct effect.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition: something upon which the existence of the ruling depends, but is not the cause.
العلامة (al-alamah): A sign: a pure indicator of the ruling without any causal connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi terminology, what is the role of a condition (shart) in relation to the ruling?
A) It directly causes the ruling.
B) It leads to the ruling without effect.
C) The existence of the ruling depends on it.
D) It merely indicates the ruling.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, every legal descriptor is classified as cause, reason, condition, or sign, each with a distinct role in the derivation of rulings.

Hanafi Classification of Attributes in Qiyas: Cause, Reason, Condition, and Sign
Definitional expansion of four categories of attributes in Qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the four categories of attributes ('illa, sabab, sharṭ, 'alāma) as per Hanafi usul and distinguish them based on effectiveness and leading.

Core Idea
An attribute attached to a ruling is either a cause (effective & inducing), a reason (leads via a cause), a condition (depends on existence), or a sign (merely indicative).

In Hanafi legal theory, not every attribute connected to a ruling operates in the same way. Understanding the precise role of an attribute—whether it directly causes, indirectly leads, enables, or simply signals—is crucial for correct qiyas (analogical reasoning). This passage presents the systematic classification from Fawatih al-Rahamut, beginning with the general definition and then delineating each category based on two criteria: effectiveness (ta'thir) and leading (ifḍā').

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching framework
The passage states that any external attribute related to a ruling is either effective and inducing (cause), or leads without effect (reason), or is effective but not leading, further divided into condition (if existence depends on it) and sign (if not dependent).
The attribute must be external (khārij) to exclude essential parts (arkān).
2
Define cause ('illa)
A cause is effective (mu'aththir) and inducing (bā'ith) of the ruling. It is the true effective cause in qiyas. Previous sections covered its types.
Effectiveness means the ruling is directly attached to it; inducing means it prompts the legislator to assign the ruling.
3
Define reason (sabab)
A reason leads to the ruling without being effective. The cause intervenes between the reason and the ruling. Example: driving an animal leads to death via trampling (the cause).
Sometimes reason is metaphorically called cause, but the technical distinction remains.
4
Define condition (sharṭ) and sign ('alāma)
If the attribute is effective but not leading, and the ruling's existence depends on it, it is a condition. If no dependence, it is a sign (purely indicative). Example: the sun's decline is a sign for Ẓuhr prayer obligation; the cause is the entry of time.
Conditions are external prerequisites; signs merely signal the ruling.
5
Apply to a classic example
Classify 'zawāl al-shams' (sun's decline). Is it a cause, reason, condition, or sign? It is a sign, as it indicates the entry of prayer time (which is the cause).
This illustrates the practical use of the classification in usul analysis.

قال الشافعي رضي الله عنه استحسان فافهم (وإن لم تضف إليه) عطف على قوله : إن أضيف أي إن لم يضف العلة إليه (فهو السبب الحقيقي كالدلالة على مال المسلم (للسارق) فسرق بعد الدلالة ( فلا يضمن الدال المسروق، لأن الدال ليس كالفاعل المختار) إذ الدلالة لا تستلزم السرقة، فإنها من اختيار السارق واختياره ليس مضافاً إلى الدلالة، فلم تضف السرقة إلى الدليل بوجه فلا يضمن ومن ثمة أي من أجل أن الدال ليس كالفاعل المختار (لا يشترك في الغنيمة من (دل) لعسكر المسلمين على حصن ولم يذهب مع المجاهدين) وإنما جاهد العسكر بأنفسهم ففتحوا فغنموا غنيمة، فليس للدال حق فيه لأنه سبب محض لا يضاف إليه الفتح، والجهاد بوجه أصلاً، هذا بخلاف المودع والمحرم إذا دلا) السارق والصائد (على الوديعة والصيد فسرق السارق الوديعة وقتل الصائد الصيد حيث يضمنان) المودع للوديعة المسروقة والمحرم للصيد المقتول وذلك لأن الدلالة على الوديعة للسارق وعلى الصيد للصائد (ترك الحفظ للوديعة (وإزالة الأمن للصيد (وقد التزماهما أما المودع فبالاستيداع،
Supplementary: The Hanafis said: The attribute (al-wasf), external to the thing (to exclude essential parts), attached to the ruling, either is effective and inducing (that is the cause, and its categories precede) or leads to it without effect (that is the reason, and it may be metaphorically called cause). Or if it is effective but not leading, then if the ruling's existence depends on it, it is the condition; if it does not depend, it must be purely indicative, and that is called the sign. As he said: 'And if it indicates, it is the sign.' Then every reason is a path to the ruling and leads to it, and the cause intervenes between it and the ruling. For if the cause did not intervene and exist, the ruling would definitely not exist, so there is no leading at all. Then if the cause is added to it, in that it necessitates the cause effective in the thing, then it is the reason in the sense of cause. [Page 357] ... like driving the animal so it tramples a human; driving does not effect the destruction, rather the trampling effects it, and it is added to it, but because of its trampling which is the cause, it is added to it, so it becomes a reason in the sense of cause. If you say: How can the [driving] be a reason for the forbidden destruction and how can it be in its position while it is permissible? He said: Driving with the condition of safety is what is legislated, not absolutely in any way. So the blood money is obligatory because he is a direct cause (mubāshir) of something that is in the sense of cause. Thus there is transgression from him in destroying a protected life, so he must guarantee it in exchange for direct causation (i.e., not the penalty of direct causation like being deprived of inheritance if the destroyer is a relative), and similar things like retaliation and expiation, except as recommended and precaution. Included is testimony: testimony to willful murder is a reason for retaliation, for it leads to it through the judge's verdict and the guardian's ability, so it is a reason that intervenes the cause. But the judge is not suitable for attributing the forbidden destruction to him, for he is compelled by law, nor the guardian's act, for he relied on the proof. Thus only the witnesses remain, if they are liars; they are a reason in the sense of cause, and they must pay the blood money if they recant because they destroyed a protected soul, so the penalty for the site of destruction is required, not retaliation, because retaliation is the penalty for direct causation and the act, and it did not occur from them. According to al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), he imposes retaliation if they say: 'We intentionally lied to kill him.' But if they say: 'We were mistaken' and the guardian of the killed claims intentionality, he (the guardian) takes an oath, and if he does, judgment is given for the heavy blood money from his own wealth. al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him) ruled this despite not disputing us in this principle, because the reason strengthened with complete intention is like direct causation in legal effect. How could it not be like direct causation? Retaliation was legislated for the wisdom of deterrence to deter people from killing. If this kind of causation is not considered direct so that retaliation is not imposed, then the purpose of deterrence fails and the door to widespread killing opens through this means, unlike placing a stone in the road, because it is not strengthened with complete intention. The Hanafi response to al-Shafi'i's statement is that retaliation is based on equivalence, as Allah says: 'So transgress against him with the like of what he transgressed against you' [2:194]. There is no equivalence between direct causation and indirect causation even if strengthened with complete intention. He (al-Shafi'i) may say: What is considered is equivalence in the intended purpose, which is achieved by both actions equally in the most complete manner. Perhaps what al-Shafi'i said is istihsan, so understand. If it is not added to it (i.e., the cause is not added to it), then it is the true reason, like indicating Muslim property to a thief: the thief steals after the indication, and the indicator does not guarantee the stolen property, because the indicator is not like the voluntary agent, since indication does not necessitate theft, for it is by the thief's choice, and his choice is not attributed to the indication. Thus theft is not attributed to the indicator in any way, so he does not guarantee. For this reason, one who indicates to the Muslim army a fortress but does not go with the mujahidin does not share in the spoils. The army fought by themselves, conquered, and took spoils; the indicator has no right to it because he is a mere reason to which the conquest is in no way attributed. This is contrary to the depositary and the pilgrim if they indicate [the thief and the hunter] to the deposit and the game: the thief steals the deposit and the hunter kills the game; they (the depositary and the pilgrim) do guarantee the stolen deposit and the killed game because indicating the deposit to the thief and the game to the hunter is a breach of custody for the deposit and removal of safety for the game, and they have undertaken that. The depositary by receiving the deposit, ...
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage expands on the Hanafi classification of attributes that relate to a ruling. The commentator presents four categories: cause ('illa) which is effective and inducing; reason (sabab) which leads to the ruling without being itself effective (like the sun's decline leads to obligation of prayer through the cause of prayer time); condition (shart) which is necessary for the ruling's existence but not effective or leading; and sign ('alāma) which merely indicates. Then the commentator introduces a sub-category: 'reason in the sense of cause' (sabab fī ma'nā al-'illa): when the reason necessitates the cause itself. The example given is driving an animal that tramples a person: the driving does not directly cause death (the trampling does), but because the driving leads to the trampling which is the cause, the driver is liable as if he directly caused it. This is because the driver's act was not inherently permissible (driving with safety is legislated, not reckless driving). The passage then applies this to false witnesses in a murder case: their testimony leads to execution via the judge's verdict and the guardian's action, but since the judge is compelled and the guardian relies on proof, only the witnesses (if lying) are considered a 'reason in the sense of cause' and are liable for diya (blood money) but not qisas (retaliation) because they did not directly kill. A dispute with al-Shafi'i is noted: al-Shafi'i would impose qisas if the witnesses admit intentional lying, arguing that a reason strengthened with complete intention is like direct causation for deterrence. The Hanafi rebuttal cites the Qur'anic requirement of equivalence: there is no equivalence between direct killing and indirect causation. The commentator suggests this might be an istihsan (equity) ruling by al-Shafi'i. Finally, examples of 'true reason' (sabab haqīqī) are given: indicating property to a thief does not make one liable because the thief's choice intervenes, unlike a depositary who breaks their trust by indicating the deposit.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): An attribute that is effective (mu'aththir) and inducing (bā'ith) of the ruling; the true cause in qiyas.
سبب (sabab): An attribute that leads to the ruling without being effective, but through a cause that intervenes. May be called 'reason in the sense of cause' if it necessitates the cause.
شرط (sharṭ): An attribute external to the thing, on which the existence of the ruling depends, but which is not effective or leading.
علامة ('alāma): A purely indicative attribute, not effective nor leading, nor a condition; it merely signals the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in the passage, which attribute is effective and induces the ruling without an intervening cause?
A) Cause ('illa)
B) Reason (sabab)
C) Condition (sharṭ)
D) Sign ('alāma)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of attributes into cause, reason, condition, and sign is based on two criteria: effectiveness and leading, with the cause being both effective and inducing.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
12 min
Reason in the Sense of Cause: Indirect Causation and the False Witness Dispute
Application of 'reason in the sense of cause' to liability cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of 'sabab fī ma'nā al-'illa' to cases of indirect causation and evaluate the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement on whether false witnesses are liable for qisas.

Core Idea
When a reason necessitates the cause itself, it is treated as direct causation for compensation but not for retaliation.

This passage applies the classification of attributes to concrete legal scenarios. The category 'reason in the sense of cause' (sabab fī ma'nā al-'illa) is introduced as a bridge between mere reason and direct causation. The driving of an animal that tramples a person and false testimony leading to execution are analyzed. The dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i on whether false witnesses face qisas reveals deep differences in how causation and deterrence are balanced.

Guided Path
1
Define 'reason in the sense of cause'
When a reason necessitates the very cause of the ruling—that is, when the cause is attributed to the reason—it becomes a 'reason in the sense of cause'. The passage provides the condition: the reason must be such that the effective cause is added to it.
This is a subcategory of reason, not a separate kind of attribute.
2
Analyze the animal driving example
A person drives an animal, which tramples a human to death. The driving is not the direct cause (trampling is), but since the driving led to the trampling and was done without the condition of safety, the driver is liable for diya. The driving is a 'reason in the sense of cause' because the trampling (cause) is added to it.
The liability is for compensation (damān), not qisas, because the driver did not directly kill.
3
Apply to false witnesses
False witnesses testify to a murder. Their testimony leads to the judge's verdict and the guardian's execution. The testimony is a reason; the judge's verdict is the intervening cause. But the judge is compelled and the guardian acts on proof, so the witnesses are the only ones who can be liable. They are considered a 'reason in the sense of cause' and must pay diya, not qisas.
This is because retaliation (qisas) is for direct causation (mubāshara).
4
Contrast the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if the witnesses admit intentional lying with the aim to kill, they are subject to qisas. He argues that when the reason is strengthened with complete intention, it becomes like direct causation for the purpose of deterrence.
The Hanafi response stresses equivalence: there is no equality between direct killing and indirect causation.
5
Distinguish from true reason (sabab haqīqī)
True reason occurs when the cause is not added to the reason. Example: pointing out property to a thief. The thief's choice is the cause, not the indication, so the indicator is not liable. Unlike the depositary who actively breaks trust by indicating the deposit.
The depositary's act is a breach of duty, so liability attaches.

Commentary
This passage expands on the Hanafi classification of attributes that relate to a ruling. The commentator presents four categories: cause ('illa) which is effective and inducing; reason (sabab) which leads to the ruling without being itself effective (like the sun's decline leads to obligation of prayer through the cause of prayer time); condition (shart) which is necessary for the ruling's existence but not effective or leading; and sign ('alāma) which merely indicates. Then the commentator introduces a sub-category: 'reason in the sense of cause' (sabab fī ma'nā al-'illa): when the reason necessitates the cause itself. The example given is driving an animal that tramples a person: the driving does not directly cause death (the trampling does), but because the driving leads to the trampling which is the cause, the driver is liable as if he directly caused it. This is because the driver's act was not inherently permissible (driving with safety is legislated, not reckless driving). The passage then applies this to false witnesses in a murder case: their testimony leads to execution via the judge's verdict and the guardian's action, but since the judge is compelled and the guardian relies on proof, only the witnesses (if lying) are considered a 'reason in the sense of cause' and are liable for diya (blood money) but not qisas (retaliation) because they did not directly kill. A dispute with al-Shafi'i is noted: al-Shafi'i would impose qisas if the witnesses admit intentional lying, arguing that a reason strengthened with complete intention is like direct causation for deterrence. The Hanafi rebuttal cites the Qur'anic requirement of equivalence: there is no equivalence between direct killing and indirect causation. The commentator suggests this might be an istihsan (equity) ruling by al-Shafi'i. Finally, examples of 'true reason' (sabab haqīqī) are given: indicating property to a thief does not make one liable because the thief's choice intervenes, unlike a depositary who breaks their trust by indicating the deposit.

Key Terms
سبب في معنى العلة (sabab fī ma'nā al-'illa): A reason that necessitates the cause itself, thus legally treated like direct causation in terms of liability.
مباشر (mubāshir): Direct agent; one who performs the act directly causing the result.
تسبيب (tasbīb): Indirect causation; causing through an intermediary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the liability of false witnesses who testify intentionally to cause a murder?
A) Qisas (retaliation)
B) Diya (blood money) only
C) Both qisas and diya
D) No liability

Key Takeaway
A 'reason in the sense of cause' triggers liability for compensation (diya) but not for retaliation (qisas), distinguishing indirect causation from direct action.

Ihsan: Condition or Sign for Rajm?
Dispute on whether ihsan is a condition or a sign for the obligation of rajm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument for ihsan as a condition for rajm and explain why women's testimony is accepted to prove it.

Core Idea
Ihsan is a condition for rajm, not a cause; women's testimony proves it because it is not effective in the hadd.

This passage examines a nuanced usul dispute: is ihsan a condition (shart) for the obligation of rajm or merely a sign (alamah)? The Hanafi majority, led by al-Sarakhsi and al-Bazdawi, argue it is a condition. However, an opposing view from Qadi Abu Zaid holds it is a sign. Understanding this distinction clarifies why women's testimony is accepted in this case, a point that tests the boundaries of evidence in hadd punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
What does it mean for ihsan to be a condition vs. a sign? Look at the passage: condition = obligation depends on it but not effective; sign = merely indicates the punishment.
2
Examine the opposing proof
The opponents argue that if ihsan were a condition, women's testimony would not be accepted because hadd punishments cannot be established by women's testimony.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis distinguish between effective causes (mu'aththir) and mere conditions. Ihsan is not effective in the hadd; it is a set of praiseworthy qualities that prevent zina, so women's testimony is acceptable.
4
Evaluate the practical implication
What difference does it make whether ihsan is a condition or a sign? The main difference is in the admissibility of women's testimony and the liability of witnesses who retract.
5
Determine the tarjih
The passage states the majority and chosen view is that it is a condition. The opposing view is from Qadi Abu Zaid and is not the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العلامة فمثلت بالإحصان وهو علامة وجوب الرجم (وعليه) الإمامان شمس الأئمة (السرخسي و فخر الإسلام البزدوي ، والمختار أنه شرط لوجوب الرجم وعليه الأكثر) وفي «الكشف ما اختاره الشيخان طريقة القاضي الإمام أبي زيد في «التقويم»، وأما أصحابنا المتقدمون وعامة المتأخرين منهم من سواهم من الفقهاء فقد سموا الإحصان شرطاً (لنا التوقف بلا تأثير ولا افضاء) أي وجوب الرجم يتوقف على الإحصان، وليس الإحصان مؤثراً فيه ومفضياً إليه وهو الشرط أتباع الشيخين قالوا : أولاً: يقبل فيه شهادة النساء مع الرجال عندنا، ولو توقف الوجوب) وجوب الحد ( عليه لم يقبل) فإن الحدود لا تثبت بشهادة النساء ولو مع الرجال. وهذا الاستدلال لو تم فإنما دل على أن الأليق بمذهبنا كونه أمارة لا أنه أمارة في الواقع، فإن قبول شهادة النساء فيه ليس مجمعاً عليه (قلنا) إنما لا يثبت بشهادة النساء ما يكون مؤثراً في الحد، والاحصان ليس كذلك وهو عبارة عن خصال حميدة) من الحرية والإسلام والنكاح والعقل (ليست) تلك الخصال (مؤثرة ولا مستلزمة للعقوبة بل مانعة عن الزنا فيثبت بشهادة النساء فصار كما إذا شهدوا في غير هذه الحالة ومن ههنا) أي من أجل أن شهادة الإحصان شهادة خصال حميدة لم يضمنوا إذا رجعوا لأنهم ما كانوا أتوا إلا بالثناء، والذي يسده عما فعل كذا قالوا والحق أن هذا الثناء أتلف نفسه بغير حق ونفسه كانت معصومة، فلا بدّ الضمان على المتعدي وصاحب العلة غير متعد وكذا القاضي في حكمه إنما المتعدي صاحب هذا الثناء الذي هو الشرط، فينبغي أن يضمنوا والله أعلم بأحكامه
As for the sign, he gave the example of ihsan, which is the sign of the obligation of rajm. (Both the two imams) Shams al-A'imma (al-Sarakhsi and Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, and the chosen view is that it is a condition for the obligation of rajm, and the majority are upon this). In al-Kashshaf, what the two shaykhs chose is the method of Qadi Imam Abu Zaid in al-Taqwim. As for our early companions and most of the later ones, except those who differed from them among the jurists, they called ihsan a condition. (We have that it is a condition with no effect or leading to) i.e., the obligation of rajm depends on ihsan, but ihsan is not effective in it nor leading to it, and that is the condition. The followers of the two shaykhs said: First, the testimony of women along with men is accepted in it according to us, and if the obligation (the obligation of the hadd) depended on it, it would not be accepted, because hadd punishments are not established by the testimony of women even with men. If this inference were complete, it would indicate that the more appropriate in our madhhab is that it is a sign, not that it is a sign in reality, because the acceptance of women's testimony in it is not agreed upon. (We said) Indeed, only that which is effective in the hadd is not established by women's testimony, and ihsan is not like that; rather it is an expression of praiseworthy qualities (of freedom, Islam, marriage, and sanity). Those qualities are neither effective nor entailing punishment, rather they prevent zina, so it is established by women's testimony, so it becomes like if they testified in other than this case. And from here, i.e., because testimony of ihsan is testimony of praiseworthy qualities, they are not liable if they retract, because they only came with praise. And that which prevents what he did? They said thus. But the truth is that this praise destroyed him without right, and his soul was inviolable, so there must be liability on the aggressor, and the one with the cause is not an aggressor. Likewise the judge in his ruling: the aggressor is only the one with this praise, which is the condition, so they should be liable. And Allah knows best His rulings.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute on whether ihsan (being free, Muslim, adult, married) is a condition (shart) for the obligation of rajm (stoning) for adultery, or merely a sign (alamah/amarrah) that indicates the punishment. The Hanafi majority, including al-Sarakhsi and al-Bazdawi, hold it is a condition. However, Qadi Imam Abu Zaid in al-Taqwim considered it a sign. The followers of the two shaykhs argue that if ihsan were a condition, women's testimony would not be accepted to prove it, because hadd punishments cannot be established by women's testimony. But the Hanafi response is that only directly effective (mu'aththir) conditions in the hadd are not proven by women's testimony; ihsan is not effective in the punishment but rather a description of good qualities that prevent zina. Therefore, women's testimony is acceptable. The passage also discusses the liability of witnesses who retract their testimony of ihsan: since their testimony was praise, they are not liable? But the truth is that they caused harm, so they should be liable. This shows the nuanced reasoning in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
الإحصان (al-iḥṣān): A legal status consisting of freedom, Islam, marriage (consummation), and sanity; it is a condition for the punishment of rajm (stoning) for adultery.
الشرط (ash-sharṭ): A condition upon which the existence or obligation of a ruling depends, but which is not necessarily effective in causing the ruling.
العلامة/الأمارة (al-ʿalāma/al-amāra): A sign or indication that points to a ruling without being a cause or condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority in this passage, why is the testimony of women accepted to prove ihsan?
A) Because ihsan is a sign, not a condition.
B) Because ihsan is not effective in the hadd punishment.
C) Because the hadd of rajm is proven by consensus.
D) Because women's testimony is accepted in all matters.

Key Takeaway
Ihsan is a condition for rajm, not a cause; its non-effectiveness allows women's testimony to prove it, illustrating Hanafi usul's nuanced view of conditions.

Definition of Ijtihad and Its Key Terms
Defining ijtihad and analyzing its key terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ijtihad and explain the significance of each key term in the definition.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is the exertion of maximum effort by a qualified jurist (faqih) to derive a probable Shariah ruling — not mere memorization.

In this passage, we explore the technical definition of ijtihad according to Hanafi usul. The author meticulously defines each component: the exertion, the jurist, the subject matter, and the level of probability. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing genuine ijtihad from lesser forms of legal activity. This lesson will dissect the definition and clarify common misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four components
The definition has four key elements: exertion (بذل الطاقة), the jurist (الفقيه), a Shariah ruling (حكم شرعي), and probability (ظني). Locate each in the Arabic text.
2
Analyze the term 'faqih'
The author distinguishes between a faqih (one who can derive from principles) and a mujtahid (one who has already derived). Notice the argument against infinite regress.
The faqih is defined in terms of potentiality, not actuality.
3
Examine the restriction 'shari'
The ruling must be from Shariah, excluding intellectual or rational matters. Why is this necessary? Because ijtihad concerns divine rulings.
4
Understand 'zanni'
Ijtihad only yields probable rulings, not certainties. The text mentions that certainties (like the five pillars) are not subject to ijtihad.
The restriction to probability prevents ijtihad from being applied to qati'iyyat.
5
Consider the objection about regress
A potential circularity is raised: if faqih implies ijtihad, then ijtihad depends on prior ijtihad. The author refutes this by clarifying that 'exertion' is broader than 'ijtihad'.
This is a key Hanafi nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة

الاجتهاد بذل الطاقة من الفقيه في تحصيل حكم شرعي ظني أقول : المراد من الفقيه من أتقن لمباديه أي مبادىء الفقه بحيث يقدر على استخراجه من القوة إلى الفعل (لا المجتهد بالفعل العالم بمسائله (كما هو ظاهر المختصر) حيث قال : والفقيه ما تقدم، ومثله في شرح البديع أيضاً ( وإلا) أي إن لم يكن كذلك، بل يكون المراد المجتهد العالم بالفعل (لزم التسلسل في الاجتهاد لتوقفه على اجتهاد سابق ، وهو متوقف على اجتهاد آخر (ولا) أي وليس المراد من يحفظ الفروع الفقهية (فقط على ما شاع الآن لأن بذل سعيه ليس باجتهاد اصطلاحاً) وإذا عرفت هذا فقد انكشف لك حقيقة ما قالوا قيد الفقيه احتراز عن بذل الطاقة من غير الفقيه وسقط اعتراض شارح الشرح أنه لا وجه له فإنه لا يكون فقيهاً إلا بعد الاجتهاد، فبينهما تلازم، مع أنه يرد عليه أيضاً ما في «التحرير» أن التلازم بين الفقيه والمجتهد لا يضر فإنّ المذكور في التعريف بذل الطاقة وهو أعم من الاجتهاد وهذا تنزل (وإنما قيد الحكم بالشرعي لأنه المقصود ههنا وبذل الطاقة في العقليات خارج عن الاجتهاد على هذا (وأما التقيد بالظني) احترازاً عن نحو الأركان الأربعة وحرمة الزنا والشرب، والغصب، من الضروريات الدينية فمبني على أن النظرية تستلزم (الظنية وقيد النظر لا بد منه، فقيد بملزومه، والاستلزام إنما هو في الشرعيات، فلا يرد النقض بالهندسيات ( لأنها ) أي النظرية (إما لضعف دلالة المتن أو السند) فإنّ الأمر الثابت من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم بالتواتر مع قوة الدلالة والأحكام فيه يفيد القطع ضرورة وفيه ما فيه) لأن مبني النظرية على الخفاء، والخفي ربما يكون قطعياً فتأمل فيه
Conclusion. Ijtihad is the exertion of maximum effort by a faqih (jurist) to obtain a probable Shariah ruling. I say: the meaning of faqih is one who has mastered its principles—i.e., the principles of fiqh—such that he is able to derive (rulings) from potentiality to actuality. It does not mean the actual mujtahid who knows its issues (as is apparent from al-Mukhtasar, where it says: 'the faqih is what has preceded', and similarly in Sharh al-Badi'). Otherwise (if it meant the actual mujtahid) there would be infinite regress in ijtihad, because it would depend on prior ijtihad, and that on another ijtihad. Nor does it mean one who merely memorizes juristic branches, as is common nowadays, because his effort would not be ijtihad in the technical sense. When you understand this, the truth of what they said becomes clear: the restriction to faqih excludes the exertion of effort by a non-faqih, and the objection of Sharh al-Sharh that there is no basis for it falls away; for one is not a faqih except after ijtihad, so there is a necessary connection between them. Moreover, what is in al-Tahrir also refutes him: the necessary connection between faqih and mujtahid does not harm, because what is mentioned in the definition is 'exertion of effort', which is more general than ijtihad, and this is a descent. The ruling is restricted to 'Shariah' because that is intended here; exertion of effort in intellectual matters is outside ijtihad in this sense. As for the restriction to 'probable', it excludes matters like the five pillars, the prohibition of zina, drinking, and usurpation—that which is religiously necessary. This is based on the fact that theoretical matters entail probability; the restriction to theoretical is necessary, so it is restricted by its necessary consequent. The entailment occurs only in Shariah matters, so the objection of geometrical (matters) does not apply, because they are either due to weakness in the text's indication or its chain. Indeed, what is established from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family and companions, through tawatur with strong indication contains definitive rulings necessarily—there is something in it. Because theoretical matters are based on subtlety, and the subtle may be definitive, so contemplate it.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition of ijtihad according to the Hanafi school. The author explains each part of the definition: 'exertion of maximum effort' (bazl al-taqah) from a 'faqih' (qualified jurist) to derive a 'Shariah ruling' that is 'probable' (zanni). He clarifies that a faqih is not someone who simply memorizes rulings but has mastered the principles of fiqh to derive rulings from evidence. The restriction 'Shariah' excludes intellectual matters, and 'probable' excludes certainties like the obligatory nature of prayer or prohibition of zina. The commentary addresses a potential objection about the relationship between faqih and mujtahid, showing no circularity.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): بذل الطاقة من الفقيه في تحصيل حكم شرعي ظني (exertion of maximum effort by a faqih to derive a probable Shariah ruling)
الفقيه (al-faqih): من أتقن مبادئ الفقه بحيث يقدر على استخراج الأحكام من القوة إلى الفعل (one who has mastered the principles of fiqh to derive rulings from potentiality to actuality)
الحكم الشرعي (al-hukm al-shar'i): الحكم الذي يقصد به الشارع (the ruling intended by the Lawgiver)
الظني (al-zanni): ما لا يفيد القطع (what does not yield certainty)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the term 'zanni' (probable) necessary in the definition of ijtihad?
A) To exclude rulings that are certain (qati'iyyat)
B) To include geometric proofs as a valid subject for ijtihad
C) Because all ijtihad leads to probable rulings, not certainties
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is the disciplined exertion of a qualified jurist to derive probable Shariah rulings, distinct from memorization or certainty.
